=== Merging Branches, Oh Boy! ===


--- What Exactly Is Git? ---

Instructor: Okay, so let's begin
with a very important question.
What exactly is Git?
So Git is what's known as a version control system,
sometimes abbreviated as VCS.
Not only is it a VCS, it's actually the world's most popular
version control system.
So what exactly is a version control system?
It doesn't sound all that glamorous.
Essentially, version control is software that helps track
and manage changes to a set of files over time.
Also doesn't sound all that glamorous or exciting,
but hugely important.
Think of companies like Facebook or Google
with huge code bases, thousands of files
and thousands of developers.
They're all working in parallel
and making changes day-to-day.
How do you manage those and track those changes
and combine them and undo changes?
That is the role of a version control system like Git.
In general, most version control systems allow things
like revisiting earlier versions of files,
comparing changes made between different versions
and different files, undoing changes,
sharing changes with other people.
Git does this and more.
We're gonna go into this in just a little bit,
but that's a general intro to this concept
of version control.
And to be clear, GIT is just one
of many version control options out there.
Over the years, they've kind of come and gone,
but some of the more well-known ones include Subversion,
CVS, and Mercurial.
And they all have similar goals of helping developers
or helping users track changes to projects over time.
But they do have significant differences in how
they operate the syntax and how they achieve those goals.
But the good news for us,
I mean, obviously, this is a Git course,
so we're gonna learn Git,
but there's a good reason to learn Git.
It is the clear winner.
If there ever was a version control system war,
Git is clearly the most popular these days.
This is the most recent data I could find
from three years ago,
2018 Stack Overflow developer survey.
Nearly 90% of respondents reported
that Git was their version control system of choice.
And the thing is, in the last few years,
this survey has just stopped asking this question.
It's abandoned it because it's just so clear.
The number is probably more like 95% these days
of developers that use Git.
So we're here to learn Git.
That's good news for us.
I'll end by just showing you what Git calls itself
on the Git homepage.
It's a free and open source distributed
version control system designed to handle everything
from small to very large projects with speed and efficiency.
It's easy to learn.
We'll see about that.
It's not bad to learn, but I wouldn't say it's painless,
and it has a tiny footprint with lightning-fast performance.
It outclass other tools like Subversion, CVS.
Oh boy, look at that, trash talking the competition.
Anyway, the stuff we care about is up here.
It is a version control system, that is Git at its core.
It helps users manage changes to projects over time.
In the next video, we'll talk in more concrete terms
about what Git actually does.

=== The Ins and Outs of Stashing ===


--- A Quick History Of Git ---

Instructor: This video, like every video in the course,
I guess, is completely optional.
But this video in particular, I wanna focus on the history
of Git just for a couple of minutes.
It's kind of fascinating in my opinion,
but it's not super relevant to learning how to use Git.
So no big deal if you skip it, but let's do it,
let's talk about the creation of Git.
So there's a very famous developer named Linus Torvalds.
It's probably closer to Linus Torvalds,
but the Americanized version is Linus Torvalds.
He is truly a legendary engineer.
He's a creator, the main developer behind Linux,
but also Git.
So very prolific, very talented,
and he's always been very pro open-source projects,
meaning, if you're not familiar with the term code bases
that are available for others to see
and collaborate on and tweak
and try their own things with, instead of hiding code
behind some sort of paywall, making it all secret.
Anyway, at some point he was working on Linux with a team
and they were using a different version control system
called BitKeeper.
This is the Wikipedia page for Git.
So BitKeeper was a tool that did basically
what Git helps you do, manage versions.
However, at some point,
I guess there was a fight, a disagreement,
and the owners, the creators of BitKeeper
did not allow the Linux team to use it for free anymore.
They wanted to charge money.
They, I guess, removed the license.
So this was controversial.
It spurred the creation
of a couple of version control systems, including Mercurial.
But for our purposes,
what we care about is that Linus was frustrated,
he was -- off about that,
he wanted a free version control system
that had features that he desired,
and at the time, nothing fit that bill.
There were paid services that had those features
and there were free ones
that just didn't have the features he wanted.
So he wanted to make something fast and open-source,
and that's actually exactly what he did.
He just said, "Screw it, I'm gonna do it on my own,"
and he spent most of April, 2005,
just writing code to make it himself.
It's a very impressive effort.
So he was unhappy with the existing tools.
He got to work, he said, "I'll do it myself,"
and that turned into Git, in just a matter of days
he had the basic functionality done.
Now, that doesn't mean that it's a one-man show
and that he's in charge of Git to this day,
there have been many collaborators.
In fact, he handed over development of Git after,
I think maybe the first month or two
to some other developers anyway,
but basically he saw a need for a product
that didn't exist as a free open-source tool.
He created it himself, and now it's a huge hit,
everyone uses it, 15 years later,
over 90% of all developers worldwide use Git
on a daily basis.
Now, one interesting note is,
about the naming, the name Git.
In development, Linus Torvalds referred to Git
as "The stupid content tracker,"
just as his own personal name,
but eventually he settled on the name Git.
And according to the official source code for Git,
you can view this online,
there are a couple of explanations
for the meaning of the name Git, depending on your mood.
So it's either a random three letter combination
that is pronounceable, it's always good,
and not actually used by any common Unix command.
The second option of the meaning of Git
is stupid contemptible and despicable, simple.
Then we have Global Information Tracker.
That's the more corporate cleaned-up version,
Git, G-I-T, Global Information Tracker.
That's for when you're in a good mood
and it actually works for you.
Angels sing and a light suddenly fills the room,
and then when things break
and it doesn't work, (chuckles)
you can see it is gosh darn idiotic truckload
of you know what.
I always feel like such a goodie two shoes in these videos.
I don't wanna lead to any angry reviews
and commenters, but this isn't the source code.
I didn't put this in there myself.
That's in the official source for Git.
So the last note that I think is kind of funny,
in British slang, it means an unpleasant person,
and Linus Torvalds sarcastically quipped
that he actually named it after himself.
He said, "I'm an egotistical bastard
and I name all the projects after myself,
first Linux, like Linus, now git,"
joking that he's an unpleasant person.
Or maybe he's a gosh darn idiotic truckload of
you know what.
So just kind of a fun little bit of history.
It's always amazing when one person goes about
trying to solve a problem and they succeed.
And then it just becomes incredibly widely used
and widespread.
That's exactly what happened with Git.
It started with one guy hunkered down somewhere,
I mean, incredibly talented developer,
it's not just like me or you most likely, (chuckles)
but a very talented developer just taking on existing tools
and trying to improve upon them
with a free open-source tool that now is everywhere, okay.


=== Undoing Changes & Time Traveling ===


--- Who Uses Git? ---

Instructor: Next up, I'd like to talk about who uses Git.

You don't have to know about this in order to use it.
But the first most obvious group would be engineers,
developers, coders,
ranging from people who work on

=== Github: The Basics ===


--- Git Vs. Github: What's The Difference? ---

Instructor: Next up, I want to talk about
an important distinction, the difference
between Git and GitHub.
This course does cover both.
They are closely related, but they are not the same thing.
So Git, as we've been talking about,
is this version control software.
It runs on somebody's machine, it runs on my laptop,
it runs on your desktop, or whatever.
It runs on people's computers.
It doesn't need internet.
You don't need to register for an account.
It's just something that you download.
If you don't have it yet, and then you use it
on your machine, you could never have internet ever
and you can still use Git,
in the same way that you could if you had like,
a text editor, or Microsoft Word, or Excel,
you can create those documents.
You don't need the internet, you don't need to sign up.
Although, I don't know, maybe these days,
Microsoft needs an account.
I don't know.
But whatever.
Text edit, you can just write text documents, whenever.
GitHub, on the other hand, is a service.
It's a web service, and what it does is,
it takes Git repositories.
That's the fancy way of saying Git projects.
And it hosts them.
It stores them in the cloud,
and makes it easier to collaborate
on them with other people.
So it's a website you do need an account for.
You do need the internet to use it,
and it takes the stuff you do locally on your laptop,
or my laptop, or whatever, using Git.
And then it allows us to put that online,
and share it with other people, or the other way around.
Other people can put their stuff online,
and then we can get their code,
or their Git projects on our machines.
So it connects people, it connects Git projects.
And in this course, we're going to start with Git first.
You kind of have to, we don't have a choice.
We have to understand how Git works
and the different commands before we can move on to GitHub.
So that will come second.
Although it's not like the course is split exactly in two,
because we do cover some Git content
after we learn more about GitHub.
So just to try and visualize this or help you visualize it,
if this is my project I've been working on locally
on my machine, no internet, no collaborators involved,
and this is my Git history,
I have a whole series of these checkpoints.
If I wanted to share this with other people
and collaborate with them, I could put it up on GitHub.
That's what I'm showing here.
There's a cloud, that's the GitHub logo.
I'm taking this work and all this history,
and putting it online on GitHub for some collaborators,
or maybe for the whole world to see.
Then those collaborators here, we've got two people.
They can take that work and get it on their machine.
They can add on, they can modify it, they can do
their own work, and then share that back up to GitHub,
and then I can go and get it from GitHub,
and we can all work together.
So all of a sudden, we can have lots and lots of people
collaborating on projects, thanks to GitHub.
That is something we will learn.
It's kind of a separate workflow,

--- Installing Git: Terminal Vs. GUIs ---

Instructor: All right, so before we go on

That's why I have this jar on the right
with some Git history inside of it.
So we can have as many repos as needed,

whatever Git commands I run, whatever history I store

We wanna keep our repositories separate,

It's not the end of the world,

Git will get confused,

Now, the next thing that's crucial to understand
about making a commit is that it's not the same
as just saving a file.
It's actually, think of it as something
that's built on top of saving files.
We have to make changes first,
and save them to our files before we can even commit.
So, we might use the term checkpoint, or save point.
But we're not talking about just saving a file.
That has nothing to do with Git.
We save files all the time.
But once we have made changes,
we can then group them together into a commit.

plus a folder called logos.

in one of these files and saved it.
And then written more code in another one and saved it.
Then I make that commit.
And the next thing that you need to know
about making a commit is that
it's actually a multi-step process.
It's not as simple as just hitting a button
that says, "Commit, commit, commit."
Instead, there's an intermediate step
where we actually call out
the particular changes that we've made
that we want to include in a commit.
And this allows us to selectively make commits,
which is really, really nice.
For example, imagine I've been working all day,
or all morning, on some project.
I spent a lot of time working on this project,
and I made changes to seven different files.
as you can see on the left hand side.
I deleted a file, I modified two files,
I created four files.
And instead of just making one generic commit
that just says checkpoint right now.
Instead, I can actually call out some of these,
and group them together based upon
the actual stuff that I did.
For example, I might group together
those first three changes.
I deleted a file called team.html.
And then I changed two files called
about.html and about.css.
Those were one thing that I did across three files.
I added some new team members to our about page.
Whatever the, you know, about page looks like.
So that was one commit.
And then, I can take these other four changes,
and group them together into a separate standalone commit.
So this is what I'm talking about
when I say there's an intermediate step.
We can select certain changes,
and make a commit with those changes.
So we don't just say, "Commit, commit, commit."
Instead, we're getting in there with tweezers,
and we're saying, "All right, this, and this, and this.
Now we're gonna commit those changes."
So, to actually make a commit,
there are two associated commands.
And we're gonna talk about the first one in the next video.
It's called git add.
And then the second command is git commit.
And we'll use git add in order to highlight,
or select, those changes that we want
to include in the upcoming commit.
So just to reiterate all of this,
a commit is a checkpoint in time.
It's a snapshot of changes in our repository.
Eventually, we'll be able to go back
to earlier commits, undo commits, merge commits.
There's a whole bunch of things that we can do.
For now, we're just focused on plotting ahead
and leaving a trail of commits
behind a trail of checkpoints.
And when we make one of these snapshots,
one of these commits, we can call out
the specific changes that we want to include in a commit,
rather than just automatically including
all changes in our project, we can select them.
That's the first step. And then commit them.
So in the next video,
I'll show you how to do that first bit.

--- Finally, The Git Commit Command! ---

Instructor: Okay, so now we're gonna talk
about the git commit command,
which is the command we actually use to make a commit
using whatever changes we have previously staged.
But before we actually run the command,
and I definitely recommend
you don't run git commit just yet.
I'll explain why.
Before we run it, we need to discuss commit messages.
Whenever we make a commit,
Git expects us to include a message
that describes or summarizes the changes
included in that snapshot in the given commit.
For example, when I grouped together these files,
the navbar changes, I staged them.
When I committed, in this example,
I added a message, "Add branded navbar."
Or here I change three files, I stage them.
I committed with the message,
"Add team members to about page."
So we'll talk more about writing good commit messages

So over here on our first novel,
the project that I've been working on,

it's :q will get you outta there.

of getting to that same destination.

You don't have to configure an editor,
you don't have to worry about Vim or another text editor.
All you do is provide your message and hit enter.

--- Keeping Your Commits Atomic ---

Instructor: One of the more common pieces of Git advice
that many a blog article has been written about
is to keep your commits atomic.
So what does that mean?
That's what I wanna talk about in this video
and I'll show you an example.
In this context, the word atomic does not mean
having to do with an atom,
but rather of something being irreducible or of a, I guess,
a base unit, a single component,
that makes up a larger system.
So if that doesn't mean anything to you,
just read the first paragraph here.
When possible,
your commits should encompass a single feature,
a single change, a fix.
In other words, try and keep each commit focused
on a single thing.
And this is important,
especially if you're working with code
and you're writing code, you're adding features
or fixing bugs.
It's super important to keep things as atomic as possible
to keep each commit focused on one thing.
Again, that does not mean one file,
that means one feature, one thing that you worked on.
So lemme show you an example.
Here, I've got my first novel repository going still,
and I'm gonna do two things this morning.
First of all, I'm gonna add some inspiration,
some visual inspiration,

with all the copyright restrictions I have to worry about.

I'll just do it through Finder, I'll call it mood board.
And then I'm just gonna put those images in there.
So now I have that folder in there and of course,
if I type git status git is gonna tell me,
hey, there's a whole new directory mood board
that I see but I'm not tracking.

Catsby in the character's file, somewhere in here, I'm sure,
here we go.
When I looked once more for Catsby, he had vanished.
So we can trust that it changed across all those files.
So now I'm ready to start committing, it's later in the day
or maybe I'm gonna take a lunch break.
So I type git status and we have a couple of things, right?
We have four files that have been modified
plus a whole new directory
that contains a bunch of files inside,
well, three image files.
So I'm not just gonna add all of this to one single commit,
that breaks this rule
or this pattern of wanting to keep each commit
focused on a single thing.
Here, it's very easy for me to break this up
and just say okay, my first commit,
the order doesn't really matter,
but one commit I'll make will be around changing
Gatsby to Catsby, that was one thing that I did.
And then the other commit that I'll make
will be around adding to my mood board
or creating my mood board.
So why don't I start by just adding chapter one
and chapter two and the characters and outline.
So notice I did not do git add dots,
'cause I don't want the mood board.
So now if I type git status,
I've got these four things staged for my next commit
and now I'm gonna commit.
So git commit.
And those four files, those four changes,
they all had to do with changing Gatsby to Catsby.
So I'd go with replace Gatsby or how about
rename Gatsby to Catsby.
Okay.
I type git status,
and we still have some uncommitted changes,
some stuff I've worked on that Git does not know about.
It's a whole directory, that's what the slash means.
So if I wanted to, I could add specific files
from inside of there, git add moodboard/,
and then I could type the name of one of those files
like rolls, it's a Rolls Royce image,
so I could do it that way,
or I can just add the entire directory like this
and I'll just add the entire thing.
Now if I type git status,
you can see all three image files have been added.
They are going to be committed whenever I commit,
so I'll commit now.
Git commit -m, what should I do?
How about start off or create mood board?
Sure, create mood board.
There we go.
So I just made two separate commits
instead of one larger commit,
following this idea, keeping our commits atomic,
breaking them down into some sort of
single, irreducible unit or piece,
instead of having like a multipurpose commit
that just includes everything you did at once,
break it down into smaller pieces.
This matters a lot when you're working on code,
especially when you're working with a team of people.
And you may need to undo some of the things that you write.
You may introduce a bug at some point
and if you boiled in a whole bunch of changes
into one commit, rolling back that commit
could undo a ton of work.
But if you kept your commits atomic, single purpose,
then if somebody needs to undo one of them,
you don't lose all that other work.
It also makes your code easier to review
if you do or when you do code reviews.
It's just generally a practice you wanna follow,
group things together in a way that makes sense.
Keep things small, but don't just break them up
to be as tiny as possible,
keep them focused on a single thing.

--- Commit Messages: Present Or Past Tense? ---

Instructor: Next up a quick video
on commit messages and which tense,
present or past tense, you should use while writing them,
and does it even matter?
This is kind of a weird thing in Git.
It always baffled me when I was starting out,
but the GIT documentation officially recommends
that you use present tense imperative style commit messages.
So present tense, you know meaning verbs
like make something or change x, fix x,
instead of past tense, fixed, changed, updated.
So imperative style though
or the imperative mood is best illustrated probably
by the quote from the documentation.
Describe your changes in imperative mood.
"make xyzzy do frotz"
instead of "makes xyzzy do frotz"
or "I changed xyzzy to do frotz."
Write things as if you're giving orders
to the code base to change its behavior.
Personally, I always found this weird.
I still do, even if I follow this a lot of the time,
it just doesn't seem natural to me.
You don't have to do this, of course,
this is all just convention, just a practice
you can get in the habit of.
There's a lot of debate over this,
but, again, this is sort of
the official recommendation from the Git docs.
Here's a blog post written by Eumir Gaspar where he talks
about the two, well, I guess there's more than two options,
but the two common options using the present tense
or the past tense, and the present tense
as we've already talked about, is what Git recommends.
And one of the main reasons people use it is
that Git actually generates its own messages at some point.
When we talk about merging,
it will generate a commit message that looks
like this, merge poll requests, blah, blah, blah.
So it says merge in present tense.
But honestly, in my opinion, I kind of feel
like past tense makes more sense
to me because you're making a commit
with the work that you have done.
It's finished, you're committing it.
But honestly, at the end of the day,
it doesn't really matter as long as you are consistent
or, more importantly,
as long as you're following the pattern set out
by whatever organization you're working with.
If it's a company or an open-source project,
if everyone else is doing present tense
and they ask you to do present tense,
you should do present tense.
So I try and stick with present tense when I'm teaching.
You know, if we type git log,
the commit messages I have been writing are, you know
split x or rename or create or begin.
So those are present tense imperative,
but the community is somewhat split on this.
If you look at this popular stack
overflow post from 10 years ago,
the top answer says you definitely should use present tense.
But then the second answer says you should
almost always use the past tense.
So that's number one and number two directly in conflict.
Anyway, this video is going on longer than it needs to.
I just wanted to make that point that
in the real world, you'll likely come
across present tense imperative, commit messages.
And as I mentioned, you don't have to follow this pattern,
especially in your own personal use.
But if you're working at a company
or on an open-source project, definitely pay attention
to the guidelines for writing commit messages.

--- Ignoring Files w/ .gitignore ---

Instructor: When you're working on a project
and you're using Git,
there are often situations
where you have files, directories
that you just don't want Git to know about
or to track at any point.
You want them to be completely ignored
and there's a mechanism for doing this.
We can create a particular file called .gitignore,
but before we go into that,
let's talk about why you might want this.
One of the most common situations
is if you're working on an application,
you're writing code where you're using API keys,
credentials, any sort of application secret
that is tied potentially to a credit card like from AWS
or some API that you're using
where you don't want those to get out into the world.
So you put them in a separate file
and if you push your code up to GitHub,
which I realize we haven't talked about,
but if you share that code and you make it public,
you don't want that file to be included.
So you'll still have the file yourself locally
but you don't want Git to track it.
Other things include operating system files.
So on a Mac there are these pesky .DS_Store files
that have nothing to do with your project or your code.
They just show up in directories.
They're system hidden files,
so you don't want to track those with Git.
There are similar files on Windows with a different name.
Other things, log files
or any files that are output by your application
that could easily be generated again.
So if you have an application when you run it,
it generates some, you know, reports every single day
and you regenerate that report on demand,
you don't really don't need Git
to track that and save that report
because you can regenerate that report, if that makes sense.
And then lastly,
a really common scenario is dependencies or packages.
So if you've ever worked with Node or Python
or really any well any language
and you work with a bunch of dependencies that you download
and install, those do not need to be tracked by Git.
That could be hundreds
or maybe thousands of different folders
and files that you can easily go and get at any point.
You don't need to bother tracking the dependencies themself.
So I'm gonna show you an example of how we do this.
As I mentioned, the mechanism is through this file
with a very particular name.
It's a hidden file that we create .gitignore
and if we put that really anywhere in a repository
but traditionally we'll put it
in the root of the repository, the top of it,
although it doesn't have to be there,
we create a file with that name.
Git will look for that file and inside of it
we can tell Git what to ignore.
So we can put exact file names.
So the first bullet point here is .DS_Store,
Git will ignore a file with that name.
We can give it folder names by putting a slash at the end.
And we can also do fancier patterns.
For example, this line here, *.log,
this is a wild card that will match anything.
I think anything except for a slash.
It will match any character
followed by the extension .log.
So this allows you to tell Git, ignore all log files.
I don't want to have to write them out.
I don't know what their names will be.
Just anything with .log.
So I'll just quickly show you some examples.

but on GitHub there are projects

--- Introducing Branches ---

Instructor: Okay, so before we dive into branching
let's just talk for like 10 seconds, probably 30 seconds
about how Git works at a very, very basic level.
When we make a commit,
each commit gets this weird looking hash,
this long series of numbers and letters
that is supposed to be unique
that corresponds to the contents
of a commit plus a couple of other things.
If you don't know about hashing functions, don't worry.
You don't need to understand any of it in order to use Git,
I'd say most people don't who still use Git daily.
I will have a video in sort of an appendix
at the end of the course or towards the end
that goes into some more details about
how the hashing actually works,
what is hashed together,
what hashing algorithm is used.
It's called SHA 1.
Anyway, all that we need to know
is that every commit has this unique hash,
this unique identifier that corresponds to the commit.
And each commit references at least one parent commit
that came before it.
So here we can see this most recent commit in blue
has a commit that came before it, this pink one,
and before that came this purplely blue one.
And that happens to be the initial commit.
It does not have anything before it.
It does not have a parent commit.
That happens the very first time you commit.
So we have this linear history
where one commit leads to the next,
which leads to the next.
But often when we're working
on a real project in the real world,
we need to work in multiple contexts simultaneously.
So imagine I'm working,
I don't know, I work at some company.
I'm working on a web application,
and I'm trying out two different color scheme variations
for a website
and I wanna try them both out
and figure out which one I like best.
And at the same time,
I also have been tasked
with trying to fix a really horrible bug.
But this bug is proving very difficult to solve.
I'm actually needing to delete some code
and comment stuff out,
move stuff around to try and hunt down the bug.
So it's not as simple as just fixing a typo.
I have to totally destroy part of the code base
to find this bug.
And at the same time,
one of my teammates is working on adding a new feature,
a little chat bot in the bottom corner.
You know, those things that pop up
and you can chat with customer service or something.
It's not clear at the moment
if our company wants to use that,
but he's going ahead and trying it
as a bit of an experiment.
And then another coworker is adding some new functionality
to the search bar, autocomplete.
And yet another developer is doing something very different.
She's doing a very experimental radical design overhaul
of our entire application layout,
but it won't be presented until next month.
And we don't even know if any of it will be used
after next month.
So, lots of different contexts.
If we all worked in a linear fashion,
one commit after the next,
this would be practically impossible.
How could we be working on separate things
some of which break other people's code, right?
If I'm trying to fix a horrible bug
and deleting a bunch of code, removing files,
I'm just making a mess.
That's gonna be a big problem
if somebody else is trying to add a new feature.
Likewise, if someone is doing
a radical experimental design change
on every single view of our application,
everything changes.
But I'm trying to work
on some minor color scheme variations,
or if someone's working on adding a chat widget
to the bottom right of our screen,
those things need to happen in separate contexts
because they have implications on one another
and they really need to happen in isolation
until maybe at some point we decide to incorporate changes
into each other's contexts.
So that is where branching comes in.
That's exactly what Git branches allow us to do.
Branches are a signature feature of Git
and you can kind of think of them
as alternative timelines for a project.
They allow us to create separate contexts whenever we want.
We can try new things,
we can work on multiple ideas in parallel,
we can experiment, we can break stuff,
and whatever we do on one branch will not impact
what happens on other branches.
Although we can decide to then combine branches.
We call that merging and we'll learn how to do that,
but that's something we have to decide to do.
Otherwise, if we make a change in one branch,
the other branches do not know about it.
They exist in isolation.
So this allows me to make a commit
and maybe make another commit on one branch.
And then at any point,
splinter off and try something.
On this pink branch up top
I'm calling new color scheme.
I could try a new color scheme out,
but over here if I switch back to this branch
none of that code comes with me,
none of those new commits.
So I could branch off again and work on a bug fix,
but again, totally separate context.
And then I could branch off even further
from this bug fix branch and branch further
to do an experimental redesign.
So now I have at least four different contexts,
1, 2, 3, and 4.
And this means that I could be working on this,
and maybe somebody else is working on this branch,
and somebody else is working here,
or maybe someone else is working
on this purplely bluish branch in the middle,
and working on things plotting ahead
while I'm still stuck here.
We're all working in separate contexts.
But then a very important part is being able to then combine
and merge branches together when appropriate.
If you work on a bug fix and you actually solve it
then you wanna merge that bug fix
into the main code base or into another branch.
So we'll learn how to do all of this in time,
but I'm gonna stop talking for now.
In this video, I just wanted to introduce
this idea of branching,
of having separate contexts.

--- The Master Branch (Or Is It Main?) ---

-: So even though we haven't really used the term branch,
we haven't made branches or referenced them in any way.
In Git, you are always working on a branch,
and we've seen evidence of this.
You've hopefully seen this before,
on branch master nothing to commit,
or it may not say nothing to commit
but on branch master something.
So in any repository, here's MyFirstNovel,
if I type git status, here we are on branch master.
And you can see the way I have my terminal configured,
it also shows me my branch that I'm on currently.
It shows master here.
That's what that is.
So when we learn how to change branches,
you'll see that updated as well on this right hand side.
So we're always on a branch,
even though we didn't decide to be on the master branch.
We didn't create the master branch.
It's just there for us.
And that's exactly what the master branch is.
It's just the default branch name.
When you make a new Git repository,
when we run git init,
automatically the branch that we start on is called master.
Now it doesn't do anything special.
It doesn't have any fancy powers whatsoever.
It's just like any other branch.
Every single possible branch you create,
they have the same abilities, the same commands.
There's nothing special about master except for the fact
that it just happens to be made for you.
Now, some people do treat the master branch
as special in their own project.
They treat it as the source of truth.
The official project branch,
the main branch where they don't wanna mess anything up.
So they treat the master branch is
like our official working code base.
And then I'll try things on other branches and if they work,
I combine them back into master.
But again, that is a choice that you make
or that specific developers make.
From Git's perspective,
the master branch is just like any other branch.
It does not have to hold the master copy of your project.
You can delete it, you can rename it.
And, in fact, I wanna talk about renaming it for a moment.
This is a topic that will come up again
a couple of times throughout the course.
The name on Git and GitHub for the default branch name
for a long time was master.
However, in 2020, GitHub announced that they were
renaming the default branch name from master to main
as part of a larger push by tech companies
to try and avoid any potentially offensive language.
So GitHub, again, just GitHub,
made this change from master to main.
With Git, however, what we're using,
we haven't touched GitHub yet,
Git the default branch name is still master
and that's why we see things like on branch master.
We didn't name it master, it's just the default branch name.
However, there are conversations happening
and that may change in the future.
And I will also show you how to change
the default branch name if you want to do this.
It's easy to do.
But I'm gonna wait until we talk
a little bit more about branches.
I don't wanna talk about renaming and changing branch names
before we even really understand what they are.
So we will come back to this.
But I wanna bring this up
because on most of the documentation, tutorials,
videos that you see out there,
you will see the term master branch
as the default branch name.
And over time, more and more people will likely adopt main.
So we'll put a pin in that for now.
And my slides are going to continue to use master
just because it is what your Git is going
to show you branch master.
But once we get to GitHub, we will use main.
Okay, so I mentioned that there's nothing special at all
about the master branch, but a lot of companies
or a lot of people treat it as your main branch
where things should always be working.
It should be the main copy
of your code base or of your project.
So you could then make a branch to try something
experimental and make some commits and test something out.
And then if you like that, you can incorporate it back
to master branch or you can abandon it
and just move on and keep working on your master branch
and just not incorporate this experimental branch.
This is an approach a lot of people use.
It's a very common workflow and we will actually
have a section on Git workflows and collaboration patterns.
But this is a very, very common one.
It's called feature branching or creating feature branches.
So you have your main or your master branch,
which acts as the source of truth,
and then you add new feature branches
and merge them back in when it makes sense.
So here's another version of the same thing.
We have Ethel and Fitz working on their own branches,
and then our master branch or the main branch
where their changes can be merged in.
But for us, at the moment, the only significance
of the master branch is that it just exists.
So next we're gonna learn how to make our own branches
and how to switch between them.
But before that,
we're actually gonna talk about this right here.
What is this thing, HEAD -> master?

--- What On Earth Is HEAD? ---

Instructor: So, there's one more concept
we need to get out of the way
before we can talk about creating branches

--- Deleting & Renaming Branches ---

Instructor: Okay, in this video,
I wanna talk about deleting and renaming branches.
So, this is something you probably won't need to do
all that often, early on at least.

or you work on a large project,
where there's tons of branches

--- An Introduction To Merging ---

Instructor: Now that we have a lot of practice
making branches and switching between them,
let's tackle merging branches.
One of the essential features of working with branches,
so the whole idea here is that
with branches we can work within the self-contained context.
We can work on different things in parallel,
but usually we want to incorporate some of
what we've done into another branch, right?
We don't just work in a separate context
and continue to work in a new separate context.
Every time we work on something,
we need to combine some of the stuff we're doing.
If you are working on a feature
and I'm working on a feature
and we work at the same company,
at some point if both of those features are,
they're good features, we wanna incorporate them.
You're going to need to merge your feature
into some branch that I can get
and I need to merge my feature
into some branch that you can get.
And then sometimes we do just abandon a branch
and we don't merge it.
But if things go well, a lot of the time
we combine work from one branch into another.
So there's a command we use called git merge.
I'll show it in just a moment.
But as I've said a couple of times,
a really common workflow is to treat the master branch
or the main branch or whatever you call it,
some people call it trunk,
whatever you call that branch.
A lot of people treat it as the source of truth
or the most stable build of an application
where you don't want to screw anything up,
you don't do any experiments on master.
So people work on a feature branch
and then that work can then be incorporated back
into the master branch if it's deemed appropriate.
You know, it's solid
and it's gone through some different checks
and people like it,
they can merge it back into master.
But the work itself is done on a feature branch.
So everybody at X company works on their own feature branch
for each feature and then potentially merges them back
into master.
So there's a couple of things that can be confusing
about merging and basically I try and boil it down
to these two principles.
We merge branches, not specific commits,
so we don't pick one commit and the second commit
and join them together.
We merge branches.
And the second piece is that we always merge based
on the current head branch.
We merge to where we are to where head is.
So here's a simple example.
I have two branches in this example,
a master branch and a bugfix branch.
And on this bugfix branch,
you can see down here I have some commits, right?
I have diverged, I've made a new branch from master,
and I've added two commits as I'm fixing this bug,
and it's now time for me to merge that work into master.
So I want these two commits.
I want all of that work available on master.
So the workflow here is to first switch
or check out the branch
that we want to merge the changes into.
We need to move over to the receiving branch.
So I'm trying to merge these commits into master.
I need to switch to master.
So that's what I do, git switch master.
Head now points to master.
And then the second step is to actually
use the git merge command.
And I'll show you an actual demo of this in the next video.
But for now we're dealing with slides.
So we use git merge and then some branch name
and that will merge changes
from this branch into the current branch that we're on.
So this right here, git merge bugfix will take the commits
from bugfix and merge them into whatever branch we're on,
which in these diagrams is master.
So we switch to master and then I run git merge bugfix.
And you can see that master is now pointing at this commits.
So it went from there to there.
Now this is a very simple type of merge.
The rest of this section deals with other types.
Here's a easier way to visualize this, right?
These colors in this diagonal line are just
to help visually separate these branches for you.
But really they're just commits
with different pointers at different commits, right?
It's just a chain of these commits,
at least with the way this branch works.
We had master,
we branched off to bugfix and did two commits.
Now all that we did when we merged was catch up.
We moved master up to that same commit.
This is called a fast forward merge
because that's all we had to do for this branch to catch up.
It just fast forwarded a couple of commits.
There wasn't additional work on the master branch.
Now sometimes that will be the case.
So there'll be additional commits over here that don't exist
on bugfix and that is trickier to merge
and that's what we're gonna spend some time talking about.
But for our first example,
we're dealing with these fast forward merges.
So you have two branches
and one of the branches just has additional commits
that the first branch doesn't have.
So to catch up,
it's just a matter of moving that pointer forward.
So that's what the git merge command does in this example.
So git merge bugfix, if we're on the master branch,
moves that pointer up to bug fix.
So now master has all that history,
it has the work that it had from before,
but also the work from bugfix.
Okay? So again, this is called a fast forward merge.
They're nice and simple.
In the next video I'm going to demonstrate one
and we'll get some practice with our playlist example.

--- Performing A Fast Forward Merge ---

--- Oh No! Merge Conflicts! ---

Instructor: Alright,
so now we get to talk about merge conflicts.
When we merge two branches, as we've seen,
Git will do its best to automatically merge those changes
whether it's a fast forward or needs to make a merge commit
as we saw in the last video,
it tries to do things automatically and so far,
it's succeeded in the two examples I've shown you.
But if we had conflicting changes, for example,
on one branch, somebody modified a file,
and on a second branch that we're merging,
somebody deleted the same file.
Or another situation would be,
if on one branch I edit one line,
77 of some file and somebody else on the other branch
or maybe it's me too, on the other branch
I edit the same line, line 77 in the same file,
Git doesn't know how to automatically merge that.
How does it decide what to keep and what to get rid of?
So this is where merge conflicts come up.
You have to manually resolve them.
It's not a big deal.
Lemme show you the process first.
When you run git merge and if there are conflicts,
you'll see something like this,
conflict and then merge conflict in some file,
automatic merge failed,
fix the conflicts and then commit the result.
So this is a multi-step process.
Git tells us there's conflicts, then we have to go
and open the files where there are conflicts and fix them.
And then we commit those changes.
So the files
where there are conflicts are actually decorated.
They have new content to indicate where there are conflicts.
So when we open up a file, we'll see this weird stuff
in there, head and then a branch name.
I will show a demo of this, of course.
So it should make more sense than it does at the moment.
So what these markers indicate is what content came
from my head branch, the branch I'm on,
the recipient branch I'm trying to merge into.
And that's indicated by the less than the little arrow head.
And then the equal sign.
So this is what I had in this diagram
on my, let's say the master branch.
This is the change I made.
And then this is from the bug fix branch
that I'm trying to merge in.
So it's my job now to delete or keep both,
figure out which part I want, which part I don't want.
It's totally up to me.
I resolve it and then I get rid of these conflict markers,
these symbols, and then I save the file and then I go back
and add and commit the changes.
So here's a step-by-step guide.
You run into a conflict,
Git tells you what files there are conflicts in.
We open up those files, then we edit the files.
We, you know, figure out how do we want
to resolve the conflict?
Do I want all three lines?
Do I want just these two or just this one?
You combine them, it's up to you, right?
And with code, usually you need to figure out what matters
and what's supposed to be there.
And you know, you have to go through it and be careful.
But the point is you are doing it as a human.
Git is not just deciding for you.
So it's very important.
We go through, we remove those conflicts,
we decide what we want to keep
and then we remove the markers as I mentioned.
We get rid of this, we get rid of this
and we get rid of this.
Save.
Now we add our changes and make a commit and we're done.
We resolve the conflict.
Okay, so I know it seems complicated
but I'm gonna show a demo in the next video.

--- Introducing The Git Diff Command ---

when you're starting out.
So the git diff command is all about showing changes in git.
So in a given repository, there are many situations
where we might wanna know what changed
between the last commits and our working directory.
What's changed between the staging area
and the working directory?
What's changed between two branches
or two different files over a certain number of commits?
What's changed between what we have locally
versus what's on GitHub?
All these different scenarios where we may want to learn
about changes, about comparisons.
So the git diff command does that.
The git diff command is powerful,
but also sometimes a bit overwhelming
when you're starting out.
It doesn't do anything to the repository,
doesn't impact anything.

--- Stashing Basics: Git Stash Save & Pop ---

Instructor: Alright, so let's learn a bit more
about how stashing actually works.
We've talked about why we've kind of motivated it.
Let's see the commands.
Fortunately for us, Git provides a command called git stash
that helps us stash uncommitted changes,
and it allows us to return to them later
without having to clutter up our history
with unnecessary commits.
We generally, I mean, you can make a commit
with stuff that you know is not quite complete
or that you're not ready to commit.
It's not the end of the world to have those extra commits,
but a lot of the time you don't wanna do that.
Commits kind of are viewed as
like the official history of your project.
And if you're just screwing around, for example,
or you're halfway done with a feature
and you just need to quickly switch over,
stash really can be helpful.
So the command git stash, just git stash,
at any point will take all uncommitted changes,
basically anything staged or unstaged,
and it's going to stash them.
It kind of hides them away, but it remembers them
and it reverts those changes in our working copy.
So we don't see those changes anymore,
but they're still available in that stash
that we can come back and retrieve them.
So the command git stash is also,
basically it's a short version of git stash save.
It's just, this is a, I guess, an alias for it.
They do the same thing.
They save your current uncommitted,
your staged and unstaged changes,
so that you can come back to them.
So it saves those changes, does not make a commit.
And then we could switch branches, just as a quick example.
And then at any point I can use git stash pop
to remove the most recently stashed changes in the stash
and reapply them to my working copy, wherever I may be.
So I can also use git stash to stash some changes
and then maybe a little while later,
reapply them to a different branch
or to the same branch that I stashed them on.
So I'll show that.
And I'm gonna show a little workflow here, some diagrams.
So let's say I'm on the bugfix branch.
You can see I've got the working directory.
Some changes I've made.
I've modified two files.
I've staged two files,
but I'm still not ready to make a commit
for whatever reason.
And then here's the actual repository with our commits.
And I'm working on fixing a bug, but then, hmm.
One of my coworkers, he needs help.
Says, "Hey, can you take a look
"at what I merged into master
"and make sure I didn't screw up everything?"
Okay, so I need to quickly hop over to master,
but I need to first stash my changes
because I'm not ready to commit them.
They conflict with what's on master.
I don't wanna take them with me.
Whatever reason, I'm gonna stash them.
So what I do is run git stash.
Git stash, or git stash save
will undo those changes.
I don't lose them,
but it reverts the changes in my working directory
and it stashes them away.
So I'm just visualizing, you know, four files here.
If I go back, we had three modified,
one that I created in green.
Same thing here.
Both the staged and unstaged changes,
they're all in the stash.
So now I can hop over to the master branch.
I can do whatever I need.
I can even make new commits on the current branch.
I can really do anything
without having to worry about those changes coming with me.
And then when I'm done, I can reapply those changes.
So, I can go back to my bugfix branch where I was,
and then call git stash pop,
which will take the changes that were in the stash,
pop them off, and add them back into my working directory
or in the staging area,
and I can keep working exactly where I was
before all of this happened.
So I'm gonna demonstrate this in the next video.
But those two commands,
git stash and git stash pop go hand in hand.
Git stash puts stuff in the stash.
Git stash pop removes the most recent thing from the stash.

--- Un-Modifying With Git Restore ---

Instructor: All right, so now we're onto a new command,
git restore,
and this is actually a new command to git.
It's pretty new, it's not all that well-known,
so there's a lot of existing tutorials and videos
and courses that don't mention it at all.
Git restore takes some of the burden away from git checkout.
It does- remember how I've said this a couple times,
git checkout does a lot of things,
and git switch alongside git restore,

--- Reverting Commits With...Git Revert ---

Instructor: Okay, we've made it
to our last command for this section.
Another similar sounding command, git revert.
Yes, we have restore,
we have reset and now revert.
They all kind of sound like they do the same thing.
Revert actually does something very similar to reset.
It has to do with undoing changes from a commit,
but there's a very important difference.
So both revert and reset will undo changes from a commits,
but the way that they accomplish it is different
as we saw with reset.
Reset removes commits entirely
and it moves the branch pointer backwards
as if those commits never occurred,
they never existed in the first place.
Revert does something different.
It actually makes a brand new commit.
And in that new commit,
it undoes the changes from an earlier commit.
So it still helps us undo changes,
but again, it makes a new commit
that basically says undoing changes
instead of just deleting everything
and deleting history, removing commits.
That can be a problem
if you're collaborating with other people.
So at the end of this video,
I'll explain why you might use revert
or reset, vice versa.
Okay, so let me just visualize that for you.
So here is a diagram of using reset
where if we reset back two commits,
we lose those commits entirely.
They're gone.
Whereas if I use revert,
let's say that I made a mistake
on this most recent commit.
It's already been committed
and I want to remove that change or changes,
but I'm gonna revert.
I end up with a new commit
and this new commit effectively undoes this one.
So I revert a particular commit hash.
This is the commit that I'm reverting.
It doesn't go away.
It's still there in the history of my repo,
but the changes from it are gone hopefully.
So let me demonstrate this.
I'll go ahead and do a really simple example.
I'll make a bad commit.
Bad commit.
And same thing, I'll do it over in cat.
Okay, so I have two changes
and I haven't made a commit yet,
but I'll commit them now, -a -m "make a bad commit".
Okay, so I have this bad commit.
We can ignore this other branch,
but on master, I have bad commits
and I realize, you know,
I really did not mean to make that commit.
I want to get rid of it.
Well, in the last video, we saw to use reset,
but I actually don't wanna reset.
I want to keep a record of this commit,
but I want to undo the changes.
So I'm gonna use revert.
So this is the commit I want to revert.
I can use the whole, you know, head tilde one,
tilde two type syntax
or I can use a particular commit hash.
So I'm gonna do git revert
and then this commit hash right there.
Okay.
And it opens up my editor, right?
Because it generates a new commit.
Just like when we merge,
it generates a commit when we merge.
Or sometimes, if it's not a fast forward.
When there's a merge commit,
we need to provide a message.
So the default message is revert, make a bad commit.
This was the commit that we reverted, right?
That was the message.
Make a bad commit.
So it's reverting that.
Now we can put other stuff in here,
maybe an explanation why we're reverting it,
what's wrong with that commit,
but I'm just gonna close it
and let's see what happens.
If we type git log oneline,
that bad commit is still there,
but now we have this new commit
at the tip of our master branch
and that new commit reverts the one before it.
If we go back to our editor,
we can see that the two changes we made are gone.
So we still have the history preserved of that commit,
but its changes have been reverted.
So why would you do this?
What's the reasoning?
Why not just reset?
Well it has to do with collaboration
which we haven't really talked about yet,
but if you're working on a team with other people
and they're all on, you know, different machines,
you don't just all work on the same laptop.
If other people have copies of that commit,
if everyone else had that bad commit on their copy
and then I decided to get rid of it using reset,
that can cause some serious problems
because I'm undoing history
that other people have
and that can be difficult to reconcile.
So instead, what we usually would do is use revert
and that will make a new commit, as we've seen
and I can share that new commit with everyone.
Now potentially, I might make a mistake
and make a commit I want to get rid of
and I haven't shared it with anyone else yet.
Then it's fine to use reset.
Nobody ever knows.
They don't have it on their machines.
But as soon as collaborators have a commit,
if you're trying to get rid of those changes,
it's better to use revert.
So here's a demonstration.
Some diagrams, I'm working with two friends
and then here's my local version of my repository.
So I'm using just commit numbers here.
So everybody has one, two, and three, right?
One, two, three, one, two, three.
And also, my friend down here has done more work.
She has commit four that she's worked on
and I don't have that
and neither does our other friend.
Anyway, I realize I wanna get rid
of those two and three commits.
I use reset and that's a problem
'cause I've already shared that with my friends.
So it's gonna be very difficult
to reconcile their code with mine
because they already have those commits
and they've been working on top of them.
I altered the history that they already have.
So instead what I should do is revert
and make a new commit
that undoes the changes from some other commit.
And that is easy for me to share with them
and for them to reconcile.
So it's kind of a simple explanation
because we haven't talked about collaborating
and sharing code and you know,
pushing and pulling in GitHub and all of that stuff.
But that's coming up very soon.
I think we're pretty much at the end
of sort of the core git functionality
and we're gonna start talking about GitHub.
The last thing I'll say about the revert command
is that sometimes reverting a commit
can result in conflicts
where you need to go into the file,
just like any other merge conflict
and figure out what you want to keep
and what you want to get rid of.
Because like our commit we just reverted was simple,
it was just get rid of a line,
but if you're reverting something from a while back
and Git doesn't know what to keep and what not to keep,
you have to do it yourself.
You've gotta go into the files
and resolve the conflicts
and then make your commit manually.
But the end result is the same.
Okay, so that's git revert.
It will undo a commit by making a new commit
that undoes the changes.

--- What Does Github Do For Us? ---

Instructor: Alright, so let's start
with the obvious question.
What exactly is GitHub or what does it do?
There's many answers to that question
because GitHub over the years
has really evolved and added on a bunch of features.
But the core mechanic,
the single thing that GitHub is known for
is that it's a hosting platform for Git repositories.
GitHub allows us to put our Git repos in the cloud.
We can access them from anywhere,
we can share them with people,
and we can collaborate with people on Git repos.
So GitHub has a bunch of features built
that have to do with collaboration
and discussion about Git repos.
So I showed this slide very early on
but I just want, I return to it.
There is an important
huge difference between Git and GitHub.
Git, hopefully you're familiar with by now
is a version control system
that we run on our machines locally.
We don't need the internet, we don't need an account.
You could go your entire life working with Git
and never using GitHub.
GitHub on the other hand, is a website.
It's a hosting platform for Git repositories.
You need the internet to access it.
You need an account.
It is completely, I don't wanna say completely separate
from Git, because it is a hosting service for Git repos,
but Git itself is completely independent.
So imagine I'm working on a video game,
a mobile game that I'm developing on my machine,
and I'm using Git to track it.
I have a couple of commits.
Let's say there's like a couple hundred.

to fit in the diagram

that repo from my machine up to GitHub.

One, I just wanna back things up.
So I keep doing my work
and I can push those changes up to GitHub.
So now GitHub has a record of my repository.
So maybe there's a house fire, a horrible tragedy.

--- Cloning Non-Github Repos ---

Instructor: One other thing about git clone,
something that I do get asked frequently from beginners
is do I have permission to clone this stuff?
The answer is yes.
If you see it on GitHub, if you actually can see it,
you have permission to clone it
and do whatever you want with it
in terms of tweaking the code.
And that doesn't mean you can turn around
and resell it necessarily, depending on how it's licensed,
but you definitely are able to clone
that repository to your machine.
So anybody can clone, if it's a public repository,
and if it was private,
you wouldn't be able to see it anyways.
You wouldn't know the URL, you wouldn't be able to go
to it on GitHub, but if you can see it on GitHub
copy that URL, you can clone it.
You don't need to be an owner or a contributor.
However, you're not allowed to just push up
your own changes, we haven't talked about pushing,
but once we get there, you can't just push changes
up to a repository that somebody else owns.
There's a whole workflow for suggesting changes,
for becoming a contributor,
but you can't just take over someone's repository,
clone it, delete everything,
and then push that up to GitHub.
So that's very, very important to understand.
If you see the repo on GitHub, you can clone it.
Now, everything I've said has been cloning
or everything I've demonstrated has been using git clone
with GitHub repositories because GitHub is so popular,
it's the standard place to host Git repositories.
But remember that git clone
works with any hosted repository.
In fact, if you go to the documentation,
the Git docs for git clone,
there is no mention of GitHub anywhere on here.
So even if that's where a lot of people are using it,
is to clone GitHub repos,
that's just how people are using it,
that is not the nature of the command.
Instead, it works with any hosted repository,
whether that's something that you're serving yourself,
like maybe a company has their own internal server
with a hosted repo,
or maybe we're using something like GitLab.
So I actually struggled to find something that was public,
that we could clone, that was not from GitHub.

--- A Crash Course on Git Remotes ---

--- What does "git push -u" mean? ---

Instructor: One more thing to talk about with git push
is the dash U option, which you've likely seen.
When you create a new repository,
which I won't, actually.

So what is this dash U option?
Well, it stands for upstream,
which probably doesn't mean a lot.
The upstream of a local branch is kind of like,
think of it as a connection pointing to a remote branch.
So if we have our master branch on our local machine,
normally we want the upstream branch to be origin master
or some master branch on GitHub.
They are connected,

without having to specify origin master,

then I get to use this shortcut.

--- Remote Tracking Branches: What Are They? ---

Instructor: So this section is focused
on getting code down from GitHub to our machine.
And this is really important when we're collaborating

whether it's one I made or I'm cloning React
or some really popular open source repository

we have at the very beginning nothing on my machine.
We have the GitHub repository.

a regular old branch reference.
And I should mention, you know, this uses master
but whatever the default branch is,
when we clone, that's what we end up with.
So whether it's main or master or trunk or purple chicken,

Whatever that GitHub repo has as its default branch
that's what we're gonna start with when we clone.

This is something called a remote tracking branch reference.

It's pointing back to the last known commit
on the master branch from the origin remote.

just like any other branch reference,
I like to visualize them as a repo,

think of it as the bookmark or a pointer
that remembers at the last time you communicated
with this remote repo.
Here is where the master branch or the main branch
or the purple branch was pointing
on that GitHub repo on origin.
So they followed this pattern remote slash branch name.
So Git names them for us.
As we talked about, origin is a very common remote name.
So we've got origin master
but that might as well be origin, eggplant or upstream.
That's another common remote name that we'll talk about.
And then Logo Design, that might be a branch.
It could be any remote name slash a branch.
But just as the example, if we clone a repo,
by default when we clone,
the remote name will be set to origin
and whatever that default branch name
in this case master will give us origin master.
So we can actually view these remote tracking branches.

--- Git Fetch: The Basics ---

Instructor: So now we're gonna start talking
about fetching and pulling.
So I'm gonna start with a hypothetical.
Here's some diagrams.
Once again, we have a local repository,
and then we have a corresponding GitHub repo,
and currently, let's say I just clone down the local repo.
I'm entirely up to date with whatever is on GitHub, right?
I have the master branch pointer and origin/master,
both referring to this latest commit,
but I go on to do some more work,
and as we saw previously, they now have diverged.
I have a commit that the GitHub repo,
the origin repo, does not have.
But I'm also working with some collaborators,
and in the meantime, while I was working
on this yellow commit, whatever that is,
those collaborators, let's just say one person,
Bob Head added three new commits
that you can see up there in green.
So those are on GitHub.
They're on the master branch.
Let's say there's just one branch.
So the master branch, they're on GitHub.
I don't have them locally.
I do have my new commit,
but I don't have those new commits coming from Bob Head.
So how do I get those changes?
This is where the two commands we're gonna discuss,
fetching and pulling, or git fetch, git pull,
this is where they come into play.
So here's a diagram that I've put together.
It's based on some really common diagrams that you'll see
where we have different locations.
They're not really physical locations,
but we've got our working directory, our workspace,
we've got the staging area,
we've got our actual local Git repository.
So something like git add, hopefully by now we know,
will stage changes, right?
git add will stage a change, puts it in the staging area,
and then git commit takes whatever is in the staging area
and then creates a new commit in the actual .git folder,
the real local repository.
And then we saw that git push will take commits
from a local repo and push them up to a remote repo,
like GitHub, or a GitHub repo, rather.
So git fetch and git pull down here
both go the other direction.
There are two commands that we can use
to get changes, new commits, from a remote repository,
from GitHub in our example.
So we're gonna start with git fetch.
Fetching is going to take remote changes,
so stuff coming from a GitHub repo,
and bring them down to the local repository,
not into our working directory.
So that is what git pull does.
I will illustrate the difference.
It can seem a little confusing at first.
Fetching allows us to download changes from a remote repo,
but those changes are not integrated into our working files.
So it's a way of kind of fetching the latest changes
and having access to them on your machine,
but without having to merge them
into our actual local working directory,
without having to mess up our files.
So think of it as this last paragraph.
Please go and get the latest information from GitHub,
but don't screw up what I'm currently working on.
So we'll have access to it using this git fetch command.
So we specify git fetch, and then a remote.
The default is origin, so you could just do git fetch,
but if you do git fetch origin,
it's going to fetch from the origin.
If you have multiple remotes, which eventually we may,
well, eventually in this course, we will,
it will fetch from whatever that remote is.
Git will basically request origin and say,
"Hey, origin, is there any changes on this GitHub repo?
If so, I want them."
We can also specifically just fetch one branch.
So git fetch origin master, or origin bugfix,
or whatever the branch is.
So here's the same situation from before, right?
We have this GitHub repo and a local repo.
I'm working on it.
I have a new commit in yellow,
and then my friend, Bob Head, I think is what I said,
added three commits to GitHub.
He pushed them up. I don't have them.
So if I don't wanna screw up my local working directory
but I want access to those changes,
I can fetch that branch, origin master,
or I can just do git fetch and get everything from origin,
and what will happen is that origin master,
remember these remote branch references, that will update.
So I have access, I can look at origin/master

I got ahead of myself.

that I fetched, right?
I don't need the internet to go look at this stuff.

fetching changes down to our local repo,

--- Git Pull: The Basics ---

Instructor: Next up we're gonna talk about git pull.
So the git pull command is similar to fetch
in that we use it to retrieve changes
from some remote repo.
But the big difference is
that pull will actually update our HEAD branch.
It updates our working directory.
Whatever changes are retrieved or pulled down
from GitHub or some other remote repo
will be integrated into our local repo
and our working directory.
So think of it as,
okay git, go and download the latest commits from GitHub
and immediately update my working directory
with those changes.
You can kind of think of it as a git fetch plus a git merge.
So fetch in the sense that we are updating
the remote tracking branch
with the latest changes from some remote.
So go and get the latest changes from, you know,
origin master or origin main or origin food.
But then second step is take those changes
from the remote tracking branch
and merge them in
or update my current branch
that I'm on with those new changes.
So the syntax looks like this,
git pull,
and then a remote name and a branch name.
What really is important to understand is that where
we run git pull from matters.
So whatever branch I'm on,
that is where the changes will be merged
into where I'm pulling down to.
So for example,
if I am on the master branch
and I run git pull origin master
it's going to fetch the changes from origin master
and then merge them into my current branch,
which if I'm on master, then it's master.
So it fetches and then merges.
And just like any other merge,
that can sometimes result in merge conflicts.
So we need to resolve those just like a normal merge.
So I'll demonstrate that,
but why don't we start with something
that won't result in a merge conflict?

When we "pull," we get those changes from the remote repo,

Where we pull from matters.
And that brings me to the last slide.
Fetch gets changes from a remote branch, or branches.
Updates the remote tracking branches with the changes,
does not merge anything into your current HEAD branch.
And it's safe to do at anytime.
It's not gonna screw up any work that you're doing.
"git pull," on the other hand,

Generally, it's a pain if you have a public repo
and you make it private.
I don't recommend, you know,
having some big established repository
with a whole bunch of collaborators

start it private so people can't see them.

all these Git repos like animals,
colors, chickens, GitHub demo novel,
I would normally make those private,
and then maybe make them public.
But I'm making them public
because I want you to be able to see them.
This is a Git course, okay.

--- What are READMEs? ---

Instructor: So, we just talked about working

I'm not gonna actually make one, one of the options
of three options here to initialize a repository

So, what is that?
README in all caps.
Well, the README is a particular file
that plays a special role on a GitHub repository.
And it's not limited to GitHub,
but in our context we're talking about GitHub.
It's a file that is supposed to explain
what your project does, whether you're working
on some API or a web application, a video game,
or even if it's just, you know,
you're a government using GitHub to make laws and legislate.
Whatever your repo is, the README file
should include information about what the project does,
what it's for, who's it for,
how to get started with the project, how to run it
and install dependencies if necessary,
why it's noteworthy, who maintains the project,
how to collaborate the project, how to get help.
Not all of that has to be there,
but if there's important information you want to convey
to somebody coming to your project for the first time,
then put it in the README file.
So, README in all caps.
You'll see them all over the place if you haven't,
and they're very important
because if you put a README file
in the root of your project,
GitHub is automatically going to display it.
It will render the contents on the page for us.

--- Introducing Github Pages ---

Instructor: So next up,

like Python, Ruby, or Node.

or documentation websites for your repositories.
