--- The Pitfalls Of A Centralized Workflow ---

Instructor: So the first of these collaborative workflows
I'm calling the centralized workflow.
It's not really an official name or anything,
but I'm telling you now, it is the weakest,
it's the worst of these workflows for collaborating.
And the reason I'm showing it to you
is that the shortcomings of the centralized workflow
are addressed by the other workflows
that we're going to discuss.
So we're kind of motivating the more complicated workflows,
but we're starting with the simplest.
So I'm calling it the centralized workflow,
aka everyone works on the master branch,
or everyone works on the main branch,
or everyone works on some single branch.
So that's why it's super-easy.
It's centralized, because all the work is done
on a single branch.
Whatever that branch is, historically,
that would just be the master branch, a default,
but it could be any branch.
So it's straightforward, right?
We don't have to worry about pushing up a whole bunch
of different branches and pulling them down
and merging different branches,
we just work with one branch.
So I have some diagrams
that I'm gonna be using throughout this section.
They all kind of follow the same pattern.
Up top, we have three different collaborators,
Pamela, David, and Forrest,
and I'm highlighting one of them
to indicate whose repository we're looking at
on their local machine versus up top in the right corner.
This is the GitHub repository that all three are using.
So this is the same repo on GitHub for everyone.
But then we have three different local repositories,
but they're all connected to that same GitHub repo.
And I have similar diagrams for the other workflows
the same characters, the same format.
So with this centralized workflow,
everybody clones the same repository,
so this is our starting point, just one commit.
So Pamela clones it down, right?
There's the master branch or the main branch,
whatever we wanna name it, it's the same branch.
So David clones it, he gets that default branch, master.
Forrest clones the same GitHub repo, and he has it.
So now they get to work.
Forrest, this guy here with a mustache
spends his day adding and committing.
He's working on some new feature.
He has two new commits.
So this is what he started with here.
He has two new commits worth of work on the master branch.
So he wants to share that with everyone, right?
He pushes that up.
So you can see those new commits.
There's now three total commits on GitHub.
During this same period where Forrest was working
and he pushed his code up,
Pamela has also been hard at work
on her own feature all day.
So that yellow commit is Pamela's new work.
Okay, so as she's been working,
Forrest already pushed some code up.
Pamela now wants to share her code with everyone.
So she wants to push it up.
So she tries it, git push origin master,
but she's not allowed to.
Why?
Well, she gets this message, failed to push.
Updates were rejected because the tip of your current branch
is behind its remote counterpart.
Merge the remote changes git pull before pushing again.
So git is telling her, I can't do that.
There are changes that you don't have on GitHub.
You need to get those changes
and merge yours with those changes,
and then you can push back up.
So already we hit a roadblock, right?
Just for Pamela to push her code up,
she has to pull down everything
that her other collaborators have pushed up.
They're all working on the same branch.
So if she does that, she pulls down
she gets the changes that Forrest had pushed
from on the master branch from GitHub,
then she merges her work in
and hopefully that goes smoothly.
There might be a bunch of conflicts, maybe not,
but she has to do it and then she can push back up.
So now there are four commits up on GitHub or more,
but a minimum of four,
although I didn't really include
there would be a merge commit here.
So let's say five, but whatever it is,
she pushes up her new commits and she's good to go, right?
That's her part done for the day.
Now, during all of this,
poor David has been working on a new feature,
but he's been having some doubts, it's not working.
He's really halfway done.
He's deleted a bunch of important files,
he's committed out some code, things are broken.
So he wants to get some input,
some advice from his collaborators.
He wants to start a discussion
and he wants them to see the code.
They're not working in an office together.
So he has this work and he has some commits
indicated by this meh face,
this I guess, is that I would call it a meh face
or a neutral face, I don't know.
Anyway, he's not happy with it, but he makes a commit.
And if we're using this centralized workflow, one branch,
so he's just working on the master branch.
So in order for him to share this,
he can't just push it up,
because as we saw Pamela earlier tried and git said,
no way, you can't do that,
you've gotta pull down, get the changes, then merge
and then you can push.
So that's what he does.
Before he can even share
this mediocre, unfinished broken code,
he has to first pull down, get all the new changes
and merge his broken stuff in, his incomplete feature,
he has to merge that in with the rest of the work
everyone else has done on the master branch,
then he can push that up
and now everyone can go and pull his work down.
And he broke the code base.
Probably if this is incomplete
everybody now has that incomplete code
and that's really frustrating and problematic.
All he wanted to do is get some input
and have some of his collaborators take a look at his work.
But to do that with this workflow
he had to wreck the master branch, the one and only branch.
So hopefully the problem is clear with those diagrams.
It's simple, you don't have to think about
where you're working if you're only working on one branch.
But there are some serious issues, some shortcomings.
First of all, every person has to spend a ton of time
resolving conflicts or at least merging code,
especially as team size scales up.
Imagine you're working with, let's say, 10 developers,
and at the end of the day,
everybody makes a bunch of commits, they push them up.
Well, for each one of those developers
to make those commits and push them up,
they first have to pull down everyone else's work
just to merge their stuff in.
So it's a lot of merging and potential conflicts
even if work is not entirely finished.
So that brings me to the next piece,
no one can work on anything
without disturbing the main code base.
How do you try some radical experiment?
How do you try removing files and committing things out,
trying to fix some crazy difficult bug
if you have to work on the master branch?
How do you experiment?
And that brings us to the last piece,
there's no way to really collaborate on anything
unless you push incomplete code up to master
and now all your other teammates have incomplete
or broken code,
which is what happened here in our final scenario.
David wanted...
Is this David?
Yes, David wanted some help.
He wanted to collaborate with, let's say, Pamela.
For her to get that code
he had to add stuff onto master that's broken,
push that up, and now the whole code base is broken
at least at the tip of the master branch.
Everyone else comes to work the next morning,
they get the most recent changes.
Oh, things are broken for everyone.
So not a good workflow.
It can be manageable if you're a very tiny team
and you're not doing anything experimental.
Even then, I wouldn't really recommend it,
but it is a option.
So that really serves to highlight the problems
with working in one branch,
which now motivates us to talk about workflows
that involve multiple branches.
That's coming up.
In the next video,
I wanna demonstrate some of these problems for real,
using a GitHub repo.

--- The All-Important Feature Branch Workflow ---

Instructor: Okay, so as we've seen
the centralized workflow, AKA
everyone works on one branch, is not ideal.
Quite a few issues.
That brings us to our next workflow,
Feature Branches.
I don't really know what to call it,
but that's the concept.
Work on feature branches.
Don't work on master, you silly goose.
That's the main idea.
If the centralized workflow is
everyone works on master,
the feature branch workflow is
nobody works on master or main.
You always work on a feature branch.
This is a very, very common pattern.
Rather than working directly on the master branch
or the main branch or any centralized branch,
all development, whether it's something tiny,
or some massive new feature, or a redesign, or a bug fix,
all development should be done on separate branches.
So part of this means treating the master or main branch
as the official project history,
the branch, right?
Like the sacred branch that you're not going to work on.
Instead, all work is done
on these side feature branches
and then merged back in
to the master or main branch when appropriate.
This allows multiple teammates to collaborate
on the same feature
without having to mess up the master or main branch.
Like with what happened
with the centralized approach
to share broken code or incomplete code
also meant breaking the master branch.
So with this feature branch workflow,
I could have a branch that never works,
it's always broken, and I can collaborate
on that with anybody else, any number of collaborators.
So my master or main branch
should not contain any broken code
unless somebody messes up
and you know thinks code is not broken
and they merge it back in and it actually is broken.
So back to our diagrams.
Here's the same three characters.
Forrest, David, and Pamela.
They all clone the same GitHub repository.
It starts with two commits and a master branch,
or a main branch.
In this diagram, master is the default branch name.
So Forrest clones it,
Pamela clones it,
and David clones it.
Everyone's at the same point.
Okay? So we work on feature branches.
David starts working on a new feature.
He's working on a branch called Add Dark Theme.
So he's hit a point where he's not totally done,
he's maybe 30% of the way there,
but he wants to share his work with the rest of the team
and specifically he wants some help from Pamela.
He wants her feedback or maybe he wants her to
write some code.
So he pushes his branch up,
he pushes add Dark theme up to GitHub.
So he didn't merge it into master, right?
It's not merged into anything.
He just pushes that feature branch up to GitHub.
So at the same time during this morning or afternoon
or whatever it is,
Pamela has been working on her own new feature,
animated scroll.
It's the name of the branch she's working on.
It's a totally separate branch,
it's in yellow here to make that clear,
she's not working on master.
Nobody works directly on master or main.
So she's working on her branch,
she's made good progress,
but then she gets a chat message from David.
David sent her a Slack saying,
Hey Pam can you please take a look
at the work that I did today on the branch?
What was it called?
Add dark mode.
Is that what it was?
Add dark theme.
Can you take a look at that branch,
pull it down from GitHub
and lemme know what you think
and add some of your own work.
So that's what she does.
She pulls down that branch.
She didn't have to stop what she was doing
and merge this into some other branch.
She can just leave her animated scroll branch
where it was if she's not ready to share it
and she just pulls down his branch.
So we've seen how to do that
and how we can switch to a remote branch.
We can fetch to see if there are new branches
that we don't have and then switch to them.
So if she gets his feature branch down,
add dark theme, and it doesn't pollute the master branch,
it doesn't pollute her animated scroll,
they can coexist side by side.
So she takes a look, she makes a couple of improvements.
She even adds her own commits on the same feature branch.
So it was three, now it's four commits.
So she pushes those up.
Add dark theme is now updated on GitHub.
She tells David,
Hey, I added some new work.
Go ahead and pull down those changes.
And so he returns to work the next morning.
I guess this was late at night or evening.
And after some Redditing in the morning he decides,
okay I guess I should get back to work.
But all that he has are these three commits
on add dark theme.
All he needs to do is pull down
see if there's any new information,
I mean he could fetch first to check,
but he gets the new work from that branch on GitHub
that Pamela did and now he can continue his work
and then at some point maybe he decides he's happy
with a new feature and he can merge it into master.
So that's what I'm illustrating here.
So that actual mechanic or mechanism
for merging into master,
usually you don't just decide
if you're at a big company, you don't decide.
All right, this goes in master.
There are usually some controls.
I mean at the very least there's code review.
Somebody or a team of people
need to look at what you've written, right?
Things need to pass tests.
So it's not a matter usually of just merging it
in whenever you want
and we'll talk about some of those mechanisms
for code approval and rejection.
But that doesn't really matter for this workflow right now.
All that matters is that when a branch is done
or when a feature is done,
it can be merged into master.
So this would ideally and, and the, the way
that this is supposed to work,
this is a fully done feature
it's not broken, it's ready to go,
then we merge it into the main branch of the master branch.
We don't merge broken stuff in
unlike the previous centralized workflow.
So everyone works in a branch on a given feature,
you could have multiple branches too.
It's not necessarily like Pamela's branch
and David's branch,
it's more one branch per feature
or branch per bug fix.
Branches are feature oriented.
So this allows us to then share those branches,
collaborate on branches,
and keep our master or main branch
as the source of truth in our repository
as the stable, always working, functioning,
non broken branch in our project.
And of course the last thing that would would need to happen
once David merges his new feature branch in,
is he would push that up so that GitHub is updated
and now everyone's master branch would include
those new commits,
those new changes that he made
on his feature branch originally.
So that's the main idea.
Master or main or whatever you want to call it,
you treat it as a branch that is not to be screwed with.
It's the official project history.
All work is done on separate branches
and then those branches can be either abandoned
or eventually merged back in to the official project history
whether that's master or main.
Remember this slide from the centralized workflow,
these were all problems with working on one branch.
No one can work on anything
without disturbing the main code base.
That's not a problem
if you're working on a feature branch.
The only way to collaborate on a feature
is to push incomplete code to master.
Nope, that's not a problem now
if everyone works on feature branches.
Way less time spent resolving conflicts and merging code
because now code only needs to be merged
when something is ready to go into master.
Not anytime anybody does literally any commits,
which is what happened when everyone works on one branch.
Okay? In conclusion, feature branches
much better approach.

Just merge them into main.

if there's no conversation or discussion

--- Introducing Pull Requests ---

Instructor: So now we get to talk about pull requests.
This is the first of a few videos on the topic.
I'm just gonna start in this video
by talking about what pull requests or PRs are
and why we want them or why they exist.
And then in subsequent videos,
we'll make our own.
Okay, so we talked about this concept of working on branches
and how that makes our life a lot easier.
But then at some point
somebody needs to merge those changes in
from a feature branch into some main branch,
master main, whatever, right?
We don't just stay on separate feature branches forever
otherwise everyone would be working in a silo
and never merging and sharing their code,
which defeats the whole collaboration thing.
So rather than just allowing a free for all merge,
especially if you're at a company where the stakes are high
and you can't afford to screw up something on the main
or the master branch, you have users, you've got,
I mean, even if not that,
if you've got a hundreds of developers
and somebody includes some bad commit
or something that breaks things on the main branch,
at the very least you've got a hundred something developers
who now have a bad version of that main branch
and they have to get rid of a commit or reset.
It's just annoying.
You wanna make sure
that whatever you are merging in
or whoever is doing the merging
has some discussion around it,
has some approval,
maybe changes are made
at the request of somebody else, right?
There's some code review process involved,
and pull requests or PRs are an integral part
of that process.
So pull requests are not a native Git feature.
They are built on top
of platforms like GitHub and Bitbucket and some others.
But really most people are referring to GitHub these days.
But again, not exclusive to GitHub
and 100% not part of Git itself.
So pull requests allow developers
to tell other team members, other collaborators
that they have some new work
that they want to be reviewed,
new work that is on a branch
that they want to be discussed, accepted,
AKA merged in or rejected.
And this facilitates discussion.
They can get feedback,
they can even update the work on the same pull request.
So think of it as,
"Hey everybody on my team, hundreds of people
I have this new branch with this feature I just completed
or I think it's complete.
It's on GitHub.
I'm making this request, this pull request.
Can you take a look at it and somebody,
will you merge it in if it's good,
or will you tell me if it's not good?"
That's pretty much what a pull request is.
So we do some work locally on a feature branch
like we did in the last couple of videos.

so that there's an opportunity to discuss
and accept or reject your changes.
Somebody makes that decision.
Maybe multiple people actually have to consent
and actually approve of your changes.

you then have to wait for it to be approved

--- Introducing Forking ---

Instructor: Okay, so we spent a lot of time
talking about pull requests,
but it's a very important feature,
and it's actually going to come up again
in this next workflow I'm going to introduce.
I'm calling it fork and clone, or forking and cloning.
It's a different approach to what we've seen so far.
So this is a workflow where,
instead of just one centralized repository on GitHub,
every developer has their own GitHub repository
in addition to some main centralized repo,
and then individual developers can make changes
and push to their own versions, their own forks,
before making pull requests.
So I'm gonna diagram all this, and hopefully it makes sense.
The whole point of this is that large open-source projects,
projects with lots of contributors,
maybe thousands and thousands of contributors,
they often employ this strategy or this workflow,
where there might be a handful
of actual maintainers and owners of the project.
They're not adding thousands of people
as direct contributors
like we saw with an invite where I could invite Stevie.
I manually went in here.
Where was that?
Manage access, and I invited Stevie.
So they're not doing that for every single person
who wants to collaborate
on React or TensorFlow or something.
Instead, this workflow, this fork and clone workflow,
enables anybody to try and make a contribution.
You don't need permission.
You make your own copy, you try making changes,
and then you make a PR.
Anybody can make a pull request.
So the first concept to understand is forking.
Forking is a feature that is not part of Git itself,
just like a PR is not part of Git.
It is a feature of GitHub
and other similar hosting services for Git repos.
When we fork a repository, it creates a personal copy
of somebody else's repository on our GitHub account.
So we would call the new copy
a fork of the original repository.
So when I click the button to fork a repo,
I'm asking GitHub to please make me
a personal copy of this repo.
This only works if it's a public repo
and we have access to it,
or, I guess if it's a private repo,
you can fork it if needed, but you still need access to it.
So I'll demonstrate this.
I wanna be clear, again, this is not a Git feature,
it's part of GitHub.
So on some repository, this is, for example,
not my repository, clearly.
Automatic Udemy course enroller,
get paid Udemy courses for free.
I don't even know if this is still up or not,
but this is not my project,
but if I wanna work on it, I'm not a collaborator.
Instead, I'm going to click this fork button right there.
So when I do that, notice what the URL or the,
what would you call this?
I guess the breadcrumb is
somebody's username slash the repository name.
When I fork, I click that button.
Now we have a copy that is on my account
called slash automatic blah, blah, blah.
So I still have all the history, all the files,
but I can do my own stuff on here.
I can clone this, I can delete branches.
I can completely break code.
It's totally up to me.
It's just my repo now,
and it's just based upon the original.
So here's the two side by side,
the original and then my fork.
It shows my username, but also,
it says where I forked it from.
And that's just an intro to the concept of forking.
In the next video, I'll demonstrate it,
and then we'll talk about how we can use this concept
to make open-source contributions.

--- The Fork & Clone Workflow ---

Narrator: So by forking,
we've made a copy of the repository
that exists on my account on Colt
and I've made some changes locally.
I pushed them up.
We see the changes.
We did this in the last video.
Where is that commit?
Basic, no, not basic.
Here it is.
Change color palette on this style directory.
Anyway, I have some work
and it actually shows I am one commit ahead
of the original repository that I forked from.
So GitHub is actually telling me automatically,
hey you have some work on your fork
that is not on the original,
and I can just ignore that
and never do anything with the original,
or I can actually share my work or attempt to share it
by making a pull request.
I can make a pull request
from my fork to the original repository.
So I'm not gonna actually go through with it
because I don't wanna bother the creator of this repository
with a dumb, useless pull request that they'll just close.
But you can see it's the same interface.
I can create a pull request with my changes.
I can leave a comment,
I can explain why.
You know,
why I think this should be merged in.
And then the repository owner
and anybody who has merge permissions
can then take my pull request and merge it in
or close it or whatever they want to do.
So that mechanism is really, really important.
This would be a way for me to make a real contribution
not this silly one that I did
but I could make a real contribution
to a project that I don't own.
I don't have permission to push to it,
but I can fork it.
I can make my own suggestions,
my own changes
and then open a pull request.
And that's the basis of this workflow.
So this workflow, I'm calling it fork and clone.
Other people say fork and clone I'm pretty sure.
It's not some official term but it's common enough.
It allows a whole bunch of people to work
on the same project without actually all having permission
and write permissions for the same repository.
So I have some diagrams that I'll be using
to illustrate how this all works.
If we have three collaborators, which is just a subset.
Let's say we have way more on a big project,
potentially thousands of people who want to collaborate.
But here on the diagram I've only included three people,
me, somebody named Taylor and somebody named Lupe.
And then we have the official project repository.
Maybe we're working on React
or some, I don't know,
some big repository that we don't have access to
to directly push or change settings,
we have to make a fork.
So we each fork, that's what the fork is representing.
And then on my local machine, I clone my fork.
So that's what this is indicating.
So I have my fork that I made from
the original project repo.
I clone that down.
So now I have this automatic remote setup called Origin.
Just because I cloned this repository,
the default remote name is going to be Origin.
So origin refers to my fork
that's done automatically when I cloned.
But then I'm gonna set up a second remote,
and this remote is going to be anything I want,
but normally we would call it upstream.
That's a very conventional name.
And it refers to the original repo that we forked.
So this allows me to basically have a place where
I can do my own work and push changes up to.
But also, if there are changes made
to the original repository
on a large open source project like React
there might be new commits and pull requests merged daily.
I want to be able to get those down on my machine.
So even if I can't push up,
I can still pull changes down.
So often upstream or original
will be the name of this remote.
But now we have two remotes.
One going to my fork and one going to the original.
So now if I do some work
I make a new commit or a bunch of commits.
Normally this would be on a feature branch
but for the diagram's sake I'm just doing it
on the main branch or whatever this branch is.
So I make some work or I make some commits.
Then if I want to try and share it
with the repository creators,
I definitely cannot push up because I don't have permission.
So instead, I can push up to my origin, which is my fork.
I can push whatever I want up there.
I can make the whole thing empty.
I have permission to do anything with that
because I own that fork.
So now on GitHub,
I have my fork that I've worked on
and I can make a pull request
just like what I showed you here where I forked 2048.
I can make a PR and ask for my changes to be merged in.
So then if I do that,
open up a pull request,
then it's up to the original
or the official project repo owners,
somebody who maintains it and who has permission
to accept to merge or close my pull request.
And if my pull request is accepted, it's merged in.
So then the next step is basically
show up at work again
and pull down my changes from upstream.
So there's new work that was done on upstream.
I don't have it locally.
So I go and get the latest from the real repository.
I pull that down and then I can do some more work locally,
push that up to my fork,
and then from my fork make a pull request when necessary.
So the whole point of this workflow is to
allow people who don't have direct access,
direct permission to push
or to change anything in a project repository.
They can still collaborate,
they can still make pull requests by forking
and then pushing their changes
to their fork and opening a PR.
So this would be the sort of cycle.
We pull down the latest changes
from the official repo,
that's one remote.
We make changes,
we push them up to our fork
and then we can make a pull request
if we want with suggestions or with our changes
that we want to be merged into the original repo.
And then we just wait and we hope that someone responds.
So here's a short summary.
Fork the project, clone the fork
add our upstream remote,
do some work,
push to the origin
and then open a PR to the original or the upstream.
So I'm gonna demo that again in the next video.
We'll wrap up by just talking about
why this is a good idea in some situations.
This is not a workflow that you may want to use
like on every single project that you you're working on,
but again, at a larger scale where there might be
some project that thousands of people
might be interested in working in,
or even if it's just two or three people
but you don't want to give them access
directly to your repository,
people can still make these suggestions.
They can make their own changes
and hope that you merge them in.
They can make a pull request from their forks.
So this allows us to work with tons and tons of people
without having to have direct collaborators added to a repo.

use rebase all the time.

to use Git rebase instead of merging.

--- Rebasing Demo Pt 2: Actually Rebasing ---

Instructor: All right, so we finally get to try
the rebase command.
I'm gonna do exactly what I'm showing you in this diagram.
Currently I have my feat branch, feature,

so I merge from master into feature,
but that gives me merge commits.
So instead I'm going to rebase,
and again, when we use the git rebase command,
we are rewriting history.
So this is the actual history of our commits.
But when I rebase, things get switched around a bit.
In fact, we get new commits generated.
So instead of creating merge commits for us,
when we use git rebase, it rewrites history,
it creates brand new commits
for each of the original feature branch commits.
So the workflow here looks like this.
I want to be on my feature branch,
and then I call, or I run git rebase master.
So this is not going to screw up
or change the master branch,
instead it's going to rewrite history
by taking my feature branch commits,
creating new ones that are based on the originals,
and putting them all at the tip of the master branch,
or starting them at the tip of the master branch,
rebasing them at the tip of this master.
So all that work,
however many commits I have on my feature branch,
that work is not gonna be destroyed or anything like that,
but the commits are going to be recreated,
and they're going to be added
to the tip of my master branch,
so we end up with a linear structure.

--- The Golden Rule: When NOT to Rebase ---

Instructor: In this video,
we'll quickly talk about why you might want to rebase,
we've kind of already touched on it,
but also most importantly, when you should not rebase,
what the golden rule is of rebasing.
So why rebase instead of merge?
Imagine this repo is way more cluttered.
Well, not cluttered, but there's a lot more history,
hundreds of commits and a lot of simultaneous work going on.
We can see with the way I've structured this very clearly
what work I did on the feature branch.

So it doesn't matter if a whole bunch
of, let's say, there's 10 or 50 or 100 other commits
from the master branch and they happen simultaneously
over the course of development
that I was working on feature.
Assuming that I get those changes,
I pull them down to master,
and I rebase onto the master branch,
my feature branch commits
are all located together at the tip.
This makes it easier for someone to review my commits
and take a look at the code base
and try and read what's going on.
In open source projects with thousands of contributors
and tons and tons of work all the time,
this makes it easier for some random person
to go read the history,
go try and understand what did this feature do, right?
I don't have to see like a million merge commits
as part of the commit history.
The end result is the same.
I guess I didn't really actually show you the code here,

coming from the master branch

So because we are updating the history,
because rebase rewrites commits, there is a big no-no,
a big thing you should be aware of,
you do not ever wanna rebase commits
that other people already have.
So you don't wanna rebase if you've pushed up some branch
to GitHub or somewhere else
and you collaborators have that work on their machine,
because then they have those commits,
and then all of a sudden you rebase
and you rewrite those commits, you rewrite history,
that is a nightmare, it's not a nightmare,
but it can be really, really annoying to reconcile
when your collaborators have different histories.
You have commits that just don't even exist in their history
and they have commits that don't exist in yours,
and it's all in the past.
That can be very tricky.
So again, the rule, never rebase commits
that you've shared with others.
So what this means is, you wanna be rebasing commits
that you have on your machine and other people don't,
so your feature branches that you're working on.
You don't wanna rebase the master branch
because people, presumably,
have that master branch or the main branch,
they have commits that you have.
So if you rewrite those commits and they're totally new,
that's a problem when you try and push up.
So you don't wanna do that,
at least that's the general principle.
So you work on a feature branch,
whatever you're doing in there, fixing a bug,
adding a new feature, whatever it is,
you can rebase your work from that branch
onto the master branch or onto another branch, right,
you're not changing the master branch.
You don't change the branch
that you are rebasing onto, right?
We didn't change the master branch here.
We only changed my commits on the feature branch.
So that's important.
The branch that you are on when you call rebase
is the one that is rebased, as we talked about.
Let's go back here.
Here I'm on the feature branch.
I rebase onto master, that's fine.
I'm not messing up master,
I'm not changing the history on master.
I'm only changing the history on my feature branch,
I'm changing those commits.

You just want to avoid rebasing commits,
rewriting history that other people have.
So once you've pushed that feature branch up,
if somebody has pulled that down, if they have that history,
if they're working on it,

--- Introducing Interactive Rebase ---

Instructor: So now we're gonna talk
about a second way of using the Git Rebase command.
We can use it to rewrite history.
We can edit commits
we can change commit message, commit messages.
We can change the contents of a commit.
We can drop or delete an entire commit.
We can even reorder things using Git Rebase.
So if you remember I had this slide
with not a peace sign, but two fingers, because
there are two main ways to use the Git Rebase command.
One is as an alternative to merging
as we've already discussed.
You're probably exhausted from that
but that's one way of using it.
But another way of using it is
as a cleanup tool and it allows us to do some
of these things I've just mentioned rewrite history.
Now of course, anytime we rewrite history
we don't wanna be doing that
on work that people already have, right?
We don't want to rewrite commits
and make new commit hashes and screw everything
up if collaborators already have that work.
But this is a common workflow.
If you've been working on a branch, you have lots
of commits that maybe you've been working on this feature
for a while and you have some kind of half baked commits
or some buggy things or half complete commits.
If you want to go through
and streamline that and combine commits down, rename them
that can be a nice thing to do
before you share that code with anyone.
So it's a step a lot of people take before sort
of the grand unveiling of their branch, of their feature.
So the way that we do this is a little bit different.
We use the Git Rebase command, but typically
we don't actually specify a branch to rebase onto.
Instead we're just going to rebase a series
of commits onto the head that they're currently based on.
So instead of rebasing onto the master branch, for example
or the main branch, we'll rebase, whatever branch we're
on my feature, we'll rebase that branch onto where
it currently is my feature.
So it's just a way of recreating commits, every commit
that we specify and we do have to provide a range.
How far do we want to go back?
In this case, four commits.
I wanna go back four commits, recreate each one,
and then we pass in this dash I flag
this dash I option it stands
for interactive, and I'll show you what that does
in the next video.
It's a very different way of rebasing than what we've
seen so far.
But remember that the Git Rebase command in the ways that
or the previous way that we saw how to use it
as an alternative to merge, what it did was it went
through all the commits on some branch and it
recreated each one right at the tip of a branch.
So it doesn't matter where in this example
but it recreates each commit from this feature branch
minus those merge commits because they're not necessary.
So each commit got a new hash, but it had the same contents
it had the same message, same commit message
but they're distinct commits.
Using the interactive mode with this dash I
it gives us the ability to instead say, as you're
making this new commit, change the name or drop it.
Don't make a new version of this one.
So this is the basic syntax.

--- The Idea Behind Git Tags ---

-: So next up we Git to talk about Git tags
which are very useful, but also fortunately
for us are pretty simple to pick up.
They're not a very complicated concept.
The main idea behind Git tags is
that we can tag particular commits.
We can label commits by creating a Git tag
a reference to a moment in time.
So these tags can be anything.
We can name them whatever we want.
But typically tags are used these days to
mark version releases for projects.
So again, think of tags as
like a label pointing to a particular moment in time
kind of like branch references, except
that a branch reference like these here, right?
These change, if we add a new commit
to the master branch, this reference moves
it points to the tip of the master branch, the next commit.
But a tag will actually stay referring
to the same commit that we set it up on.
So on the Git docs, they explain this
Git has the ability to tag specific points
in repositories history as being important.
Think of it as like a sticky note on a commit.
Typically people use this functionality to
mark release points, but again, you don't have to
but that's the context I'm gonna demonstrate it in.
It's what you'll see the most.
Really the only thing I ever see tags used for.
So tags allow us to do things like this.
Everybody, whoever sees this repository
I'm tagging this one, commit as our version 3.1 0.0 release
for whatever this software is that we're making.
So that is always going to be there no matter
how much more work we do
thousands of more commits in the master branch.
Lots of merges.
This will always be 3.1 0.0.
It's tagged that way.
Yes, we could move the tag, but we have to do it manually
unlike with a branch reference, which automatically updates.
So I can tag different points
as different releases in our history.
And all I'm doing is marking them as important moments.
And a lot of the time this happens, right?
You, you would have a new version,
a new release when some code is merged
into the master branch.
If we're working on a big project
a big open source project, lots
and lots of contributors, at some point, some code is merged
in and whether it's a bug fix or a whole new refactoring
or some radical change, that typically marks a new release.
So that's why I'm marking
or I'm tagging these merge commits
but that's not always how it works.
All that I want you to understand for now is
that a tag is just a pointer to a commit a moment in time.
It doesn't have to be in the master branch.
You can tag any commits.
And when you tag that, commits, that tag stays there.
It refers to that moment in time.
And these names can be anything.
Most of the time we're talking about versioning
but you could name them Hotdog Pickle and Bluebird Tuber
if you wanted to.
We have complete control over what we tag things as.
So there are two different types of tags
that we're going to discuss. There are lightweight tags.
Lightweight tags are simpler, they're lightweight
they're just like a name and a label.
That's it.
Think of what you see here.
A name referring to a moment in time.
Annotated tags include additional information
including a tag message, kind of like a commit message.
The author's name and email, the date.
And for that reason
because they include all this additional metadata
they are generally preferred in a lot of big projects.
They'll ask you to use annotated tags, not lightweight tags.
So I'll show you how to do both of them.
They're very simple.
It's really just one command we need to cover
which is Git tag and then we'll go on from there.
But before we go any further
I actually wanna talk a little bit about versioning
because tagging and versioning are kind of tied together.
That's the main use case for Git tags.
I wanna talk about semantic versioning
and that's what we're gonna cover in the next video.

--- A Side Note On Semantic Versioning ---

Instructor: So as we talked about in the previous video
the most common, really the only use case these days,
well, I shouldn't say only,
by far, the most common use case though for tagging
is in versioning, in marking release points
for some piece of software.
The Git docs mention this.
Typically people use this functionality
to mark release points.
It is really the thing tags are used for.
So in this video,
we're not gonna talk about tags specifically,
we're not gonna see any commands,
but I wanna talk about semantic versioning.
Semantic versioning is a protocol, a specification,
a set of rules and requirements,
that dictate how version numbers are assigned
and incremented,
basically the meaning of these three numbers
with two dots between them.
You've probably seen this system before,
it's really widespread these days,
and it is tied tightly to the usage of Git tags.
So the whole point here of semantic versioning,
it's an actual specification that we can take a look at.
It's a nice and short document,
not bad compared to some of the other
software specs out there.
And the idea is just to lay out this set of rules
for making versioning transparent and consistent
and meaningful so that these release numbers
actually have meaning encoded in those three digits.
So the system is three numbers with two dots between them,
and each spot indicates something different.
So on the far left, we have major releases,
then we have minor releases in the middle,
and then patches on the right.
So typically if you start out development
of some application or some library that you're creating,
the initial public facing release will be 1.0.0,
and I believe that's actually codified in here somewhere.
Let's see, version 1.0.0 defines public API, yes,
so that's one of the rules here, there's a couple of rules.
Then, once you have that initial release out,
small changes, patch releases,
are going to be indicated here on the far right.
So patches do not contain new features
or significant changes, definitely no breaking changes,
often just bug fixes and minor changes
that don't impact how people use the code
or the piece of software.
So if people are already using 1.0.0,
and they're comfortable with it,
if you release 1.0.1, that's a patch,
and you shouldn't change anything
that those people are using, you shouldn't surprise people
or break anything that they're doing.
So patches are small and they happen pretty frequently.
So that's what this number indicates here,
and you increment that by one for every patch,
so there could be another patch and it would be 1.0.2.
Then we have minor releases.
So minor releases signify new features,
new functionality that's been added,
but everything is still backwards compatible,
no breaking changes.
So typically, new functionality,
new features will be optional in a minor release.
It's just like, hey, here's some new stuff,
but you don't have to completely reconfigure
everything you've done as a user.
You don't have to rewrite code, but you could add this in.
So that's what minor releases are for.
And whenever there's a minor release,
you're supposed to reset the patch number back to zero.
So then you could have 1.1.1, 1.1.2, 1.1.3.
But then as soon as there's a new minor release, 1.2.0.
And then we get to major releases,
major releases are for significant changes
that are not backwards compatible,
features may be entirely removed,
changed substantially.
It will often include breaking changes.
So there's a big difference
between a minor release and a major release.
And the protocol is that for a major release,
you increment that number
and then you set the other two back to zero.
So that's the basic concept.
It's pretty simple, right?
The system, here's just three numbers,
but it makes it clear to any user or consumer
of some piece of software,
how significant a change is in a new version,
which is always very important,
especially if you're relying on a piece of software,
a library, as a dependency for your own software
that you're building.
So we can take a look at some examples,
for example, Bootstrap.
Bootstrap, if you're not familiar,
I've mentioned it a couple times in this course,
just as demos, very popular CSS framework,
component library, whatever you wanna call it.
And on GitHub, we can actually go and click on tags,
which again, we haven't seen how to make tags yet,
but then we can click on releases right here.
And we can see a whole bunch of releases,
so currently we're on 5.0.0.
By the way, v technically is not part
of the semantic versioning spec,
but most of the time you'll see a v
before those three numbers.
And also afterwards, you can append things like beta,
I believe they talk about it somewhere on here.
Yeah, a pre-release version may be noted
by appending a hyphen, and then some other stuff afterwards.
Anyway, if we keep scrolling down
we can see these different releases, here are two examples.
We have 4.5.1,
and then the next release is a patch, 4.5.2.
So this number increments,
and there's really not a lot happening here.
It looks like it's removing one line of styles
and then restoring some mixin.
So two minor things, no breaking changes.
And we can see, you know, the rules were followed, I guess,
in this versioning, in this release number,
increment that far right digit.
And then here's another example for React.
This is their change log on the React homepage.
And if we take a look at something like 16.12,
going to 16.13, this is a minor release,
but there are some changes,
some new features, it looks like.
Generally more functionality compared to a patch,
we can find a patch somewhere, like right here,
this is a patch release, as is this.
Tiny little changes, refactoring, bug fixes.
Then here we've got a minor.
This is, let's see what came before,
looks like this is a patch right there.
16.8.6, and then a minor release, 16.9.0,
we've reset that patch number to zero.
And then somewhere we've got 17.0.0,
that is a major release,
you can see they kind of make a big deal out of it.
They write a blog post on how to even update to it,
what the main changes are,
and there's a lot more stuff here.
So that's the main concept behind semantic versioning.
The version numbers, those three digits are in this case,
you know, it's what, four digits.
But those three slots separated by periods tell you a lot.
They indicate the severity or the scope of a given release
and its changes.
So let's get back to tagging in the next video.

--- Comparing Tags With Git Diff ---

Instructor: So just like with branches,
we can actually check out particular tags.
The main difference, though,
is that when we're checking out a tag,
a tag is referring to a a particular commit, not a branch.
There's a a big difference there,
where a branch reference is always going to be updated
to refer to the tip of that branch.
We check out that branch.
We're not in detached head.
But if we instead check out a particular tag,
we are going to go into detached head,
because we're basically just checking out a commit,
just like if we actually check out a particular commit
like git checkout 532FB, whatever that commit hash is,
that is gonna put us into detached head.
Same thing when we check out a tag,
because a tag is just pointing to a commit.
So it's not a problem.
If we want to go back
and actually work on an older version...
Usually, you don't just make changes to an old release.
The point is that a release is set in stone.
When it's released, it doesn't change.
That's what new releases are for
if you need to make changes.
But let's say we wanted to go back and work on
or work off of some earlier release.
How about, sure, 15.3.1.
All right?
All that we have to do is git checkout that right there.
15.3.1.
So that's a tag name.
If I try and use checkout normally,
we check out a branch or we check out a commit hash.
That's not exactly what one looks like.
Or we can check out a tag.
And we see our favorite message,
that we're now in detached head state.
You can look around, make experimental changes, commit them,
and we can make a branch if we want to keep working.
So I could do that.
I could do a git switch -c, MY,
I don't know what I wanna call this, BRANCH_FROM_TAG.
I don't know why I'm using underscores here.
Normally, I wouldn't do that.
But anyway, I now made this branch
based upon that particular commit, which was 15.3.1.
That's what the tag referred to.
And we probably won't be able to tell much difference
here at all.
But that brings me to the next point,
which is that we can actually diff different tags.
So I think a more obvious diff to do
would be between two patches.
So what about this?
17.0.0, so that was a major release,
and then there was a patch.

--- Replacing Tags With Force ---

Instructor: If, for some reason,
we need to move a tag,
so we need to have it refer to a different commit,
we can use the -f option to force that tag through.
Because, as I'll show you,
git does not like it if we try and reuse a tag.
So I've got,
let's just take this tag here, 17.0.3,
and for whatever reason,
it's a bad idea and you should not do this typically,
but let's say I wanted to,
actually, I made a mistake.

So, I can copy that commit hash,
and then I want 17.0.3,
so I can do git tag,
and then v17.0.3,
and then this commit hash.
But then git says, "Not so fast, fatal,"
always so dark sounding,
that tag already exists.
So, tag names do need to be unique.
I can't reuse that, so that's a problem.
But if I want to move that,
which is what I'm trying to do,
I made a mistake for whatever reason,
I want that tag to actually apply,
I want it to refer to this commit right here instead,
I use the -f flag.
So, just like that.
It doesn't have to go at the end of course, anywhere,
but -f and I force it through.
It says, "All right, all right, updated that tag."
It was referring to this commit,
now it refers to that commit you wanted.
And if I do my git log, mm, there it is.
It refers to this commit instead of that one.
So, that's how you can move tags if needed.
Not something I do very often at all
but if you need to, use the -f flag.

--- A Crash Course On Hashing Functions ---

Narrator: So to fully understand how Git works
we need to take a small side trip away from Git
to talk about hashing functions.
If you're familiar with the topic, the concept of hashing,
you could skip this or maybe just skim it,
we'll get back to Git specifically in the next video.
But of course, this is all connected.
So let's get going.
So you've probably come across,
I don't know why I said probably,
you have definitely come across
these long strings of characters.
They are hexadecimal characters, exactly 40 digits long.
If you're not familiar with hexadecimal,
it's base-16 instead of base-10 decimal
for a numbering system.
So base-16 means we run out of digits that we use,
at least in the decimal system, zero through nine,
so then we move on to letters A, B, C, D, E.
And F, I forgot F.
Okay, so we end up with these very long strings,
which are numbers
and they're all exactly 40 characters long.
We've seen them before.
I've used the term commit hash or just hashes.
So what exactly are those?
Well, they're the output
of something called a hashing function.
So hashing functions are a family
of functions that have one goal.
They take inputs of arbitrary size
meaning inputs of a variable size, small, large
and then spit out outputs of a fixed size.
So in this example, whatever this hashing function is,
it spits out, I'm not sure how many characters this is,
but whatever it is, you can see that both outputs
are exactly the same length, same number of digits.
It doesn't have to be hexadecimal.
Any number can be turned from hexadecimal to decimal
or to binary, right?
Numbers are numbers.
This is just one way of representing them.
They would be a little bit longer
if this was a decimal number.
Anyway, it's a fixed size.
So the input here is a sentence.
The input here is three characters.
The input could be a file potentially,
or it could be a single number,
and it spits out fixed size outputs.
So you may have come across hashing functions
in computer science contexts,
talking about data structures like hash tables,
or you may have come across the term
cryptographic hash functions,
which is a subset of hash functions.
They do the same thing, right,
they map data of some arbitrary size
to an output of a fixed size,
but they have a couple of additional constraints.
And this group of hashing functions
have a whole bunch of different applications
in information security, digital signatures,
cryptocurrencies, as well as Git.
So I'll just run through some of these really quickly.
Probably the most important one to talk about upfront
is that cryptographic hash functions
need to be deterministic,
meaning that the same input always yields the same output.
In other words, if we go back to this example,
"I love chickens" should always yield this output,
whatever it is, if that's the input,
it should always yield that output.
"LOL" should always yield this output.
So whatever the output is, it should be consistent.
There shouldn't be any variability
in what you get for the same input.
Another constraint is that a cryptographic hash function
should be a one-way function,
meaning that if you have the output
it shouldn't tell you anything about the input.
And along those lines, you shouldn't be able to guess
or infer anything about the input.
You shouldn't be able to reverse engineer
if you have the output.
So if I were to implement a password login authentication
for users on a website,
we use a hashing function to store
the hashed output of a password.
Let's say these are two passwords.
If somebody gets access to our database, a hacker
a bad actor, they see these password hashes,
they shouldn't be able to infer anything
about the actual passwords.
So that's important in a lot of situations.
Also, a small change in an input
should yield a large change in the output.
It goes along the same lines, right?
I shouldn't be able to guess.
"I love chickens" yields this.
I should not be able to guess
what "I love chicken" will yield.
They shouldn't be connected.
And then also, it should be unlikely to find two outputs
with the same value.
We want to avoid collisions whenever possible.
So mathematically, with these hashing functions
it is possible to have collisions, of course,
but the probabilities are so low
that we really don't need to worry about it.
Anyway, those are the basic rules
of cryptographic hash functions.
Now, we're talking about Git here.
We're not talking about cryptography,
we're not talking about authentication or cryptocurrencies.
But Git uses one of these hashing functions called SHA-1,
although this actually is set to change eventually.
It's something that the group behind Git has said
we plan on changing, we don't know when,
and it's gonna be a nightmare to change
but we plan on it at some point.
But at the end of the day,
there will be some replacement
that will be just another hashing function.
And this one, SHA-1 has been around for a little while
and it's a hashing function that always generates
40 digit hexadecimal strings.
So the output size is fixed, right?
We've talked about that.
And all of the hashes we've seen
are the output of the SHA-1 algorithm.
So there are a handful, more than a handful,
but there's a set of known
or commonly used cryptographic hashing functions,
including the SHA family, but there are others.
MD5, ECDSA, is that what it is, ECDSA?
I can never remember.
Yes, ECDSA.
Anyway, there's a group of these algorithms
that are most commonly used.
I just wanna end this video by demonstrating

So I've selected SHA-1 here,

--- Git As A Key-Value Datastore ---

--- Retrieving Data With Git Cat-File ---

-: So, now that we've seen how to store data
in a very low-level way in Git.

and Git hashes things for us,
but we can manually tell it,
"Hash this, turn it into a Git object, and store it."
So, we've done that a couple of times,
and we got these keys back.
Right, we have this one and this one.
So, imagine that I don't know what was stored in there,
or it was a really long file that I stored.
I want to retrieve that data out.
I have the key,
and Git is storing the information in these files.
I just can't read them.
So, to get it out, there's a function,
or there's a Git command called "cat-file,"
"git cat-file," and then we pass in an object-hash.
So that could be a commit hash,
or it could be one of these hashes.
This is not from a commit;
it's from something called a blob.
We'll go into more detail.
But any of these hashes we get in Git
they all are tied to a Git Object, one of four types.
And we can get information,
we can read them out using git cat-file.
Now this -p option tells Git
to pretty-print the contents of the object
based on the type of object it is.
So, I could pretty-print a commit
if I had a commit, using -p,
it would detect it to commit and print it out as a commit.

These are not commits.
So I can copy the whole hash and do a git cat-file -p,
and then I'll pass in the whole thing just to start.
And what do you know?
It says, "Hey, I have something stored for that.

--- Deep Dive Into Git Objects: Trees ---

Instructor: Okay, so in the previous video,

if nothing changed in those files,
the contents are the same.
A blob stores the contents of a file.
So right now Git is storing this version of the file
in one blob and then it's storing this version of the file
in another blob.
And then the trees will reference one of those versions
depending on what was in that snapshot
when I made the commit.
So I'm hoping maybe some of this is starting to make sense.
If not, you know, the good news is you could still use Git
and not have to worry about this.
But this is about as far as I want to go
in exploring all of this.
There are other things we can do.
We can actually make commits and generate trees
in the same way that we created blobs from scratch.
But I think this is a good place to stop.
Okay?
So every commit includes a reference to a parent,
unless it's the first commit, there is no parent.
And then a tree,
so that's another type of Git object we've seen.
The tree will contain a bunch of references potentially,
to other trees, if it's a subdirectory or to blobs.
And a blob contains file contents.
So a tree is the place where we store
or Git stores the file name corresponding to a blob.
And then everything's hashed.
Blobs get hashed, trees get hashed, commits get hashed.
And Git uses those hashes to easily determine
if something has changed.
Because you change a single character in a file,
that blobs hashes different.
You change something in a directory,
that tree's hash is different.
But also it uses those hashes as keys
so that it can easily retrieve things, right?
Keys are supposed to be unique in a key value data store.
So if I say "Git, give me the information
from this commit, FA4."
It can easily go and find that.
Or give me the information for this blob.
We usually don't do that on a blob basis, but Git does that.
Git will restore contents in a directory
based upon the tree in a commit.
So when I switch branches,
it's going to look at the tip of a branch,
what's that commit?
Okay, what's the tree in that commit?
Let's take that and we're going to restore
the files and the working directory
based upon the state in that tree.
So that's kind of the underlying workflow
that Git goes through.
Now we're skipping a lot of stuff,
but that's the basic idea,
with Git objects, with hashing, commits, trees, blobs.
There's also this fourth type, the annotated tag.
We have talked about tags and annotated tags.
Not a big deal.
As this image loads here. (chuckles)
They're not as important right now,
to understanding how all this works
but it's another type of Git object.
They're hashed, they're stored in the objects directory.
They include metadata, who made the tag, the dates,
a tag message, just like a commit, except that a tag
is just a pointer to a particular commit.
It is not an actual commit itself.
So it kind of stores a reference to a commit.
And a commit stores a reference to a tree
and a tree stores a references to blobs.
Alright, I applaud you if you made it through all of this.
I hope some of it made sense.

--- The Limitations of Reflogs ---

Instructor: Now before we go any further,
I think in the last video I said I was gonna show you
this get Git reflog command, but first I just need
to make a very important announcement about reflogs.
I made a separate video to tell you this.
Reflogs are only local. That's the first bit.
So I could be working on a repository
with thousands of collaborators like this React repository.
This has thousands of commits
from at least I think 1,500 different people.
I don't have their reflogs.
I don't know what happened to their head
on a day-to-day or minute-to-minute basis.
That would just be absolutely overwhelming.
So reflogs are local.
Git is only storing, it's only logging my changes
to references on my machine locally.
And, furthermore, they are non-permanent.
They do expire, and I think the default
according to the Git docs is around 90 days
where old entries will be removed.
But you can actually change that.
I don't really think it's worth changing.
I wouldn't change it, but there is a way to do that.
You can make it shorter if you wanted to,
but the main takeaway from this video
is that they do expire.
So it's not a permanent record
of everything you've ever done
if you have a really old repository
with lots of work locally.
So, again, only local changes are tracked in reflogs,
and they do expire, okay.
